* Subject:.*CS\ *100.*

:0
* Subject:.*CS\ *228.*

From seniorr at aracnet.com  Mon Aug  1 17:47:57 2005
From: seniorr at aracnet.com (Russell Senior)
Date: 01 Aug 2005 17:47:57 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] vacation utility
In-Reply-To: <42EEADE2.8010704@dimstar.net>
References: <20050801172455.GA1035@mercycorps.org>
	<42EEADE2.8010704@dimstar.net>
Message-ID: <868xzl6u6a.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Russ" == Russ Johnson <russj at dimstar.net> writes:

Russ> A good example of why vacation utilities are out of favor
Russ> arrived in my inbox today.

Russ> Sending vacation notices to mailing lists is considered bad
Russ> form.

So use one that doesn't do that.  I've used a qmail-ified vacation
program (a perl program) that specifically does not reply if:

     * The sender address includes the string -REQUEST at .
     * The sender is you.
     * The sender's name is any of:
          + daemon
          + postmaster
          + mailer-daemon
          + mailer
          + root
     * The sender matches any of the mail addresses listed in the
       optional files ~/.vacation.aliases and ~/.vacation.noreply. See
       the FILES section below for more details on these files.
     * There is a Precedence: bulk or Precedence: junk header.
     * There is a Mailing-List: header.
     * The email is flagged as SPAM by spamassassin. This will only
       happen if spamassassin is installed on the system receiving the
       email.
     * Your mail address, or any address you have listed in the optional
       ~/.vacation.aliases file does not appear in either the To: or Cc:
       headers. This feature can be disabled using the -j option. See the
       below for more details on this option.
     * An automatic reply has already been sent to the same address
       during the last week. The timeout value may be changed using the
       -t option. See the section below for more details on this option.
     * -n was specified on the command line and the user does not have a
       ~/.vacation.msg file.

I seem to recall in my pre-qmail days that the plain-jane
/usr/bin/vacation program was smart enough not to reply to mailing
list traffic either.


-- 
Russell Senior         ``I have nine fingers; you have ten.''
seniorr at aracnet.com

I'll put the question first, and then, if you're inclined to read the 
boring details, they'll follow.

How do I switch the wine user back from root to rsteff?

Here's how I got into trouble:

On the machine I recently upgraded from SuSE 9.1 to Suse 9.3, I was 
fiddling with wine. (I had used wine under 9.1 previously.) By mistake, 
I typed:

wine --help

in a root terminal. wine happily went about changing who gets to run 
wine from my rsteff user to root. It told me the following:

-----------------------
dhcppc1:~ # wine --help
wine: creating configuration directory '/root/.wine'...
Converted windows dir to new entry HKCU\Environment "windir" = 
L"c:\\windows"
wine: '/root/.wine' created successfully.
Wine 20050211
-----------------------

Then, I switched to an ordinary terminal as user=rsteff and tried the 
same command (unfortunately, I don't have enough lines retained in the 
terminal display to get the first handful of what happened):

-----------------------
Created symlink /home/rsteff/.wine/dosdevices/com1 -> /dev/ttyS0
Created symlink /home/rsteff/.wine/dosdevices/com2 -> /dev/ttyS1
Created symlink /home/rsteff/.wine/dosdevices/com3 -> /dev/ttyS2
Created symlink /home/rsteff/.wine/dosdevices/com4 -> /dev/modem
Created symlink /home/rsteff/.wine/dosdevices/lpt1 -> /dev/lp0
Created symlink /home/rsteff/.wine/dosdevices/a: -> /media/floppy
Created symlink /home/rsteff/.wine/dosdevices/c: -> 
../%HOME%/.wine/fake_windows
Created symlink /home/rsteff/.wine/dosdevices/m: -> /media/cdrom
Created symlink /home/rsteff/.wine/dosdevices/x: -> /tmp
Created symlink /home/rsteff/.wine/dosdevices/y: -> ../%HOME%
Created symlink /home/rsteff/.wine/dosdevices/z: -> /
Created symlink /home/rsteff/.wine/dosdevices/a:: -> /dev/fd0
Created symlink /home/rsteff/.wine/dosdevices/m:: -> /dev/sr0

You can now remove the [SerialPorts], [ParallelPorts], and [Drive] sections
in your configuration file, they are replaced by the above symlinks.

Converted drive type to new entry HKLM\Software\Wine\Drives "A:" = L"floppy"
Converted drive type to new entry HKLM\Software\Wine\Drives "C:" = L"hd"
Converted drive type to new entry HKLM\Software\Wine\Drives "M:" = L"cdrom"
Converted drive type to new entry HKLM\Software\Wine\Drives "X:" = L"hd"
Converted drive type to new entry HKLM\Software\Wine\Drives "Y:" = 
L"network"
Converted drive type to new entry HKLM\Software\Wine\Drives "Z:" = 
L"network"
Converted temp dir to new entry HKCU\Environment "TEMP" = 
L"C:\\Windows\\Temp"
Converted path dir to new entry HKCU\Environment "PATH" = 
L"C:\\Windows;C:\\Windows\\System;X:\\;Y:\\"
Converted profile dir to new entry HKCU\Environment "USERPROFILE" = 
L"c:\\windows\\Profiles\\Administrator"
Converted windows dir to new entry HKCU\Environment "windir" = 
L"C:\\Windows"
Converted system dir to new entry HKCU\Environment "winsysdir" = 
L"C:\\Windows\\System"
Warning: the specified Windows directory L"C:\\Windows" is not accessible.
Warning: the specified System directory L"C:\\Windows\\System" is not 
accessible.
Wine 20050211
-----------------------

Then I tried to run the version of notepad found in 
/home/rsteff/.wine/fake_windows/Windows; wine complained as it loaded 
it. The two warnings about directories that are not available (same as 
above) repeated, followed by a request to:

-----------------------
Please use the registry key HKEY_CURRENT_CONFIG\Software\Fonts\LogPixels
to set the screen resolution and remove the "Resolution" entry in the 
config file
-----------------------

Notepad loaded, and I was able to type in it, but when I tried to save, 
it complained five times that it couldn't create the directory:

'L"C:\\windows\\profiles\\rsteff\\Desktop"'

and after a bit of fiddling, Killed itself.

I switched back to the root terminal and discovered that it was looking 
for the .wine directory in /home/rsteff, and if I run notepad.exe from 
there, it works and saves just fine.

So, I'm assuming there's a configuration file somewhere that defines 
where "L" points, and that it's now pointing somewhere other than where 
it pointed before. Any ideas where?

TIA

in ~/.logout and have the system shut down when the user logs out?

On Tue, 2 Aug 2005, Rich Shepard wrote:

> On Tue, 2 Aug 2005, Rich Shepard wrote:
>   I was wrong -- it's not working. Back to the books ...

   I'm stuck.

   I've googled for insight, read the sudo section of Carla's book, read the
man pages for sudo and sudoers, tried what I thought the instructions say and
it still isn't working.

   When I try to run 'sudo halt' at the prompt I'm asked for a password, then
told that the user is not allowed to run sudo on that host.

   Here's the /etc/sudoers file from the laptop:
-------------------------------------------
# sudoers file.
#
# This file MUST be edited with the 'visudo' command as root.
#
# See the sudoers man page for the details on how to write a sudoers file.
#

# Host alias specification
Host_Alias 	LOCALHOST= localhost

# User alias specification
User_Alias 	USERS = pamela

# Cmnd alias specification
Cmnd_Alias 	SHUTDOWN = /usr/sbin/halt

# Defaults specification

# User privilege specification
root	ALL=(ALL) ALL
pamela	localhost= NOPASSWD: /sbin/shutdown -h now
USERS	LOCALHOST= NOPASSWD: SHUTDOWN

# Uncomment to allow people in group wheel to run all commands
# %wheel	ALL=(ALL)	ALL

# Same thing without a password
# %wheel	ALL=(ALL)	NOPASSWD: ALL

# Samples
# %users  ALL=/sbin/mount /cdrom,/sbin/umount /cdrom
# %users  localhost=/sbin/shutdown -h now
-------------------------------------------------

   In Pamela's ~/.logout I put the line,
sudo /usr/sbin/halt

   Obviously, I've missed something important in /etc/sudoers but I've no idea
what it is. I'd greatly appreciate a clue.

Thanks,

Rich

Message-ID: <1123003012.15373.8.camel@thoth.oit.pdx.edu>

What does your .logout have in it?  Your sudoers seems fine, but in
your .logout you should have something like: "sudo /sbin/shutdown -h
now" or "sudo /usr/sbin/halt".  You have to specify the explicit path
since you declared it within your sudoers file.

On Tue, 2005-08-02 at 09:32 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:

>    Obviously, I've missed something important in /etc/sudoers but I've no idea
> what it is. I'd greatly appreciate a clue.


Cmnd_Alias	SHUTDOWN_PAMELA=/sbin/shutdown


pamela		ALL=(root) NOPASSWD: SHUTDOWN_PAMELA


I pretty sure those two lines will give access to the shutdown command
to pamela without password.

We use something similar, in combination with ssh keys, to do some
default admin access settings for newly built Personal Telco Nodes.

A-

Did you do your edits with visudo?  /etc/sudoers can be
whitespace-picky if you do it yourself.  If not, try checking it with
visudo -c

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Tue Aug  2 10:49:21 2005
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Tue, 2 Aug 2005 11:49:21 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] Logrorate: checking whether a file is open with prerorate?
In-Reply-To: <200507300045.j6U0jOZf019274@m5p.com>
References: <20050729235558.1F7BD44CB@ulke.nakedape.cc>
	<200507300045.j6U0jOZf019274@m5p.com>
Message-ID: <f4468cae05080210494d001c78@mail.gmail.com>

This makes a blind assumption that the next run will be safe to
compress, and that this one isn't... kind of a big oversimplification
that won't always be right.

If for instance this was a monthly archive, all the archives would
always be a month behind, no?  Not to mention that there's the chance
(more likely on shorter-interval rotates), that the file will STILL be
open and being written to by some application... it just puts off the
incorrect behaviour until the next run.

This didn't seem to fit the bill for me, in fact I've never used the
delaycompress option because it doesn't seem to make much sense :)

/eli

On 7/29/05, Elliott Mitchell <ehem at m5p.com> wrote:
> >From: Eli Stair <eli.stair at gmail.com>
> > I've gotta support a bad-bad-bad application that doesn't simply
> > append data to its log, it keeps the file open in memory, and writes
> > to it "from the last known offset into the file, according to its last
> > write"... according the the brilliant developer.
> >
> > The issue is that I cannot use the create (move and create new
> > logfile) OR truncatecompress options... can't move it because it's
> > open for writing constantly (canNOT bring down the app), and when the
> > truncatecompress option is used, on the next write by the daemon the
> > "OLD" data that was removed becomes zero bytes up to the next write
> > offset:
> 
> The obvious one would be to use "delaycompress".
> 
>

From ehem at m5p.com  Tue Aug  2 16:33:45 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Tue, 2 Aug 2005 16:33:45 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Logrorate: checking whether a file is open with prerorate?
In-Reply-To: <20050802190003.7E0D543E2@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200508022333.j72NXjwD058382@m5p.com>

>From: Eli Stair <eli.stair at gmail.com>
> This makes a blind assumption that the next run will be safe to
> compress, and that this one isn't... kind of a big oversimplification
> that won't always be right.
> 
> If for instance this was a monthly archive, all the archives would
> always be a month behind, no?  Not to mention that there's the chance
> (more likely on shorter-interval rotates), that the file will STILL be
> open and being written to by some application... it just puts off the
> incorrect behaviour until the next run.

You've really got a program that will keep the log file open for a full
month, and cannot be caused to close() and then re-open() it? If this
really is so, then you'd have to write a program that acts similar to a
pipe. Make a FIFO inode and read from that. Generally SIGHUP causes most
programs to reopen files.

> This didn't seem to fit the bill for me, in fact I've never used the
> delaycompress option because it doesn't seem to make much sense :)

Makes it handy if you want to do post-mortiem examination of them,
knowing they're not going to be touched.

for enterprise users who expect some things in a product that the Open
Source projects don't generally provide. The company stays true to the
Open Source ethos. They do not attempt to impose a lock-in model on Open
Source as some vendors have. The same version of software that they
support is available from their web site with no strings attached over
the original Open Source licenses. Thus, we have to maintain excellent
service because customers can go elsewhere.  We announced our Java stack
today, and have had a *AMP stack out for a while.

Date: Wednesday, August 3
Time: 8:30-10:30pm
Place: Kells Irish Restaurant & Pub, Ulster Room, 2nd Floor
112 SW 2nd Ave., Portland, Oregon 97201


    Thanks

    Bruce Perens

From athlonrob at axpr.net  Wed Aug  3 18:33:32 2005
From: athlonrob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Wed, 03 Aug 2005 18:33:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Used Hardware
In-Reply-To: <20050803174744.GG2703@bavariati.org>
References: <1123089375.13270.3.camel@einstein.seansdomain.org>

Aaron Burt wrote:
> Aaron, who'd consider buying into a UML "condo" somewhere.

If by that you mean a shared box where everybody is in a UML... let me
share my experience with such a thing.  :-)

Three friends and I decided to get just that a while back... a beefy box
we'd all share via UMLs.  We got the box, set it up and stuff... and
thought about the shared root situation.  Since we're all decent
sysadmins, it wasn't a big issue, but we did lay down some ground rules
and procedures for things.

As we got setting up the box for various things (we expected the host to
run Apache, Postfix, and BIND for a few things) we started to find less
and less a need for the UMLs.  Now, probably eight months later...
*none* of us have gotten so far as to set up a UML yet.  We're all
happily sharing services with each other.

Rob

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Tue Aug  2 18:50:44 2005
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Tue,  2 Aug 2005 18:50:44 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Blacklist in Browser?
In-Reply-To: <20050725155818.GA21900@bavariati.org>
References: <B0012023866@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
	<20050725155818.GA21900@bavariati.org>
Message-ID: <1123033844.42f022f4873fb@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

Quoting Aaron Burt <aaron at bavariati.org>:

> On Mon, Jul 25, 2005 at 08:40:43AM -0700, Charles Sliger wrote:
> > Looking for a browser that lets you maintain a blacklist.
> 
> Blacklist of what?  You ain't paying me enough to read your mind, man.
> 
> > I would like to be able to maintain the blacklist in much the same way
> that
> > bookmarks are maintained.
> 
> If you mean a blacklist of websites, I'm sure you could use Adblocker
> in FireFox, and there may even be an extension especially for the
> purpose.  Of course, someone who's even mildly technical (e.g. nearly
> anyone younger than 18) would probably be able to find a way around it.
> See: https://addons.mozilla.org
> 
> If you need a real web-filtering system, use DansGuardian, either on
> the box as a daemon or on a separate router.  (I think IPCop supports it.)

I'm against Dansguardian.  It's a neat idea, but you can't always go through
a proxy and it has blocked the wrong sites on me many times.  A lot of web 
sites can't be accessed through a proxy at all and proxis are difficult to 
set up.  A more sensible approach for a blacklist is to intercept dns packets
and evaluate the URL in them.  If that URL is on a blacklist, send back a
response that points to a local browser instead.  All one needs to do is 
run a local dns server that supports blacklisting and perhaps dynamic 
updates, then force the local users to use it.  This approach isn't a proxy
solution, it's a dns one.  Not to mention, you could restict what web sites
can be accessed to those on a trusted list.  Apparently there is a supposed
to be a way to implement something like this using netlink and xinetd.
A more elegant solution is a blacklist record much like the A record
in bind.

-------------------------------------------------
This mail sent through IMP: http://horde.org/imp/

From seniorr at aracnet.com  Wed Aug  3 22:19:04 2005
From: seniorr at aracnet.com (Russell Senior)
Date: 03 Aug 2005 22:19:04 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] passive FTP failing ...
Message-ID: <86ek9a2saf.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


I am running an anonymous ftp server on a public IP.  Recently,
passive-mode FTP transfers are failing.  This seems to include
browsers that seem to do passive mode by default, but I can replicate
the behaviour with /usr/bin/ftp by ensuring passive mode is on.  With
passive mode off, it works just fine.  My iptable rules look like
this:

    /sbin/iptables -A ftp-input -p tcp --syn --dport 21 -j ACCEPT
    /sbin/iptables -A ftp-input -m state --state ESTABLISHED,RELATED -j ACCEPT
    /sbin/iptables -A ftp-input -p tcp --syn -j DROP
    /sbin/iptables -A ftp-input -p udp -j DROP
    /sbin/iptables -A ftp-input -j ACCEPT

It is possible the failure started when I converted from 2.4.x to
2.6.11.x.  I am going to look at the kernel possibility.  Any other
pointers?


-- 
Russell Senior         ``I have nine fingers; you have ten.''
seniorr at aracnet.com

Russell> possibility.  Any other pointers?

Never mind.  I found it.  I had ip-conntrack-ftp compiled as a module
but hadn't loaded it.  I loaded it by hand and plopped it in
/etc/modules and I should be fine now.  Sorry for the bother.

  mplayer -dumpaudio -dumpfile audio.mpg

I think the audio file comes out as mp3, or whatever compressed-audio
format DVDs use.

got a new lease for a different network.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Thu Aug  4 09:54:47 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Thu, 04 Aug 2005 09:54:47 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] (no subject)
In-Reply-To: <5.1.0.14.2.20050804074529.027cf990@www.hotsushi.com>
References: <5.1.0.14.2.20050804074529.027cf990@www.hotsushi.com>
Message-ID: <42F24857.2040909@madboa.com>

On 8/4/05 8:53 AM, Mike Neal wrote:
> Hi,
> 
> Trying to help a student connect to Comcast via a cable modem:
> 
> NETMASK=255.255.248.0
> IPADDR=24.20.92.89
> GATEWAY=24.20.88.1
> 
> It appears the gateway is on a different network. Is that allowed?

Yep.

Your concept of "different network" is perhaps too heavily influenced by 
the current custom of using 8-bit netmasks (i.e., 255.255.255.0, 
resulting in "class C" network segments) just about everywhere.

It wasn't always so. It used to be that universities with an entire 
class-B network (e.g., 130.50.0.0/255.255.0.0) used to put the 
255.255.0.0 netmask on *every* machine. Broadcasts were sent to 
potentially thousands of machines. And the default gateway was almost 
certainly on a different "class C" network than your workstation.

The size of a network is pretty arbitrary. It needn't be X.Y.Z.0-255. 
Using netmasking it can range from the entire IPv4 address space (mask 
0.0.0.0) all the way down to a single host (mask 255.255.255.255).

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

No.. that's a /21 mask which allows for 2K addresses.

router's IP.

From charlie at schluting.com  Thu Aug  4 09:59:45 2005
From: charlie at schluting.com (Charlie Schluting)
Date: Thu, 04 Aug 2005 09:59:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] (no subject)
In-Reply-To: <42F24857.2040909@madboa.com>
References: <5.1.0.14.2.20050804074529.027cf990@www.hotsushi.com>
	<42F24857.2040909@madboa.com>
Message-ID: <42F24981.6040601@schluting.com>

On 8/4/2005 9:54 AM, Paul Heinlein wrote:
> 
> The size of a network is pretty arbitrary. It needn't be X.Y.Z.0-255. 
> Using netmasking it can range from the entire IPv4 address space (mask 
> 0.0.0.0) all the way down to a single host (mask 255.255.255.255).

No.. if the netmask is 0.0.0.0 your OS will try to ARP for *everything* and 
if proxy-arp isn't enabled on the router you're using, you won't get to 
anything that isn't in the local subnet, because the entire Internet won't 
hear your ARPs.

-Charlie

No! :-)

That's a *very* broad netmask, allowing addresses from 24.20.88.0 to 
24.20.95.255.

You were probably thinking of a netmask like 255.255.255.248, of the 
sort often handed out by ISPs to customers that don't need a ton of 
publicly routable IP addresses.

   Why might a change in motherboard chipsets require the hard drive to be
reformatted? In all the years I've built my own hardware (starting in 1984)
I've not heard this before, and I've moved disks from system to system.

   The context for this question arises from the sudden death of my server's
power supply this afternoon. No warning. I cannot say that it was getting
progressively louder over time, it always seemed loud to me. First time it
was fatally afflicted.

   So, off I drove to ENU to buy a replacement power supply; an Antec 'cause
they're good quality and relatively quite quiet.

   When I put the new drive in the box and turned on the power, the system
told me, "No CPU fan. Should I shut down now? (Y/N)". Of course, I said yes.
Checked all the wires and saw nothing wrong. After going through this a few
times I pulled the box forward and watched: sure enough the fan was spinning
merrily away. After the POST the beeping and message went away.

   I called ENU and was told that the motherboard was obviously not toast, but
something must have been upset when the powersupply went. The fellow on their
end of the conversation asked me which board and chipset I had; they have
another MSI board with a similar chip set, but not the same. He told me it
was important because one "usually has to reformat the hard drive if the chip
sets are different."

   A bunch of you are hardware gurus so I'd like your opinion of both the
statement that a different chip set could require a hard drive reformat
(apparently this is more of a Microsoft issue, but even so ...) and whether
the report of no CPU fan is a transient event about which I should not worry.
Candidly, if I don't have to strip the box and swap system boards I'd prefer
to save the time and money. However, if that's the prudent thing to do I'll
do it this weekend.

   What sayith y'all?

Rich

Rich Shepard wrote:

>   Wow! So if a Microsoft box suddenly goes down the entire OS must be
> re-installed? That's crazy!

It's "possible" to not have to do that with Windows.

However, because the drivers for each piece of hardware is installed 
individually, it would be like changing motherboards after optimizing 
your kernel and removing all the excess modules.

It may work, but it definitely won't be optimized without reinstalling a 
bunch of drivers, and even then, you'll have things scattered all over 
the hard drive that aren't needed.

On Thu, 4 Aug 2005, Russell Senior wrote:

> Have you googled for the error message?

Russell, et al.:

   I think that I have found the reason why the motherboard reports the CPU
fan not running when the system first boots: it's running too slowly to be
detected.

   The new power supply is an Antec "True Power" model that adjusts fan speeds
(power supply, case, cpu) based on temperatures. When the system first boots,
the CPU temperature is sufficiently low that the fan runs slowly. However, it
probably quickly warms and the warning message stops.

   Looking at the smartd records in /var/log/messages show the CPU temperature
between 102-112 C. So I guess everything is working well and I'll save the
money, time, and hassle of replacing a working motherboard with a new one.

Rich

From rsteff at comcast.net  Fri Aug  5 17:26:01 2005
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Fri, 05 Aug 2005 17:26:01 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Macros in Thunderbird?
Message-ID: <42F40399.2030905@comcast.net>

Is there a feature in Thunderbird that would allow me to create a macro 
that I can apply to specific message? The examples I have in mind are 
s-p-a-m and fraud. I have developed a fairly simple procedure that I 
apply to every message that I identify as s-p-a-m, and another, similar 
procedure to every message I identify as fraud:

Either I, or Thunderbird itself put the offending message in the Junk 
folder.

I determine if it is s-p-a-m or fraud.

If s-p-a-m, I forward it to missed-spam at comcast.net.

If fraud, in addition to missed-spam at comcast.net, I also forward it to 
reportphishing at antiphishing.org and to uce at ftc.gov.

Then, I delete the message from the Junk folder, go to the Trash folder, 
and delete it from there, and then go to the Sent folder and delete the 
associated message from there.

Since the process is always the same, it seems logical that I should be 
able to automate it. Does anyone know if this sort of thing is available 
in Thunderbird, and if so, what it's called?

TIA

--

Dick Steffens
http://home.comcast.net/~rsteff/

From alan at clueserver.org  Fri Aug  5 19:33:42 2005
From: alan at clueserver.org (Alan)
Date: Fri, 05 Aug 2005 19:33:42 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] [OT] Hardware Question
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0508041934550.15219@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0508041934550.15219@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <1123295622.3432.23.camel@dagon.fnordora.org>

On Thu, 2005-08-04 at 19:45 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>    Why might a change in motherboard chipsets require the hard drive to be
> reformatted? In all the years I've built my own hardware (starting in 1984)
> I've not heard this before, and I've moved disks from system to system.
> 
>    The context for this question arises from the sudden death of my server's
> power supply this afternoon. No warning. I cannot say that it was getting
> progressively louder over time, it always seemed loud to me. First time it
> was fatally afflicted.
> 
>    So, off I drove to ENU to buy a replacement power supply; an Antec 'cause
> they're good quality and relatively quite quiet.
> 
>    When I put the new drive in the box and turned on the power, the system
> told me, "No CPU fan. Should I shut down now? (Y/N)". Of course, I said yes.
> Checked all the wires and saw nothing wrong. After going through this a few
> times I pulled the box forward and watched: sure enough the fan was spinning
> merrily away. After the POST the beeping and message went away.
> 
>    I called ENU and was told that the motherboard was obviously not toast, but
> something must have been upset when the powersupply went. The fellow on their
> end of the conversation asked me which board and chipset I had; they have
> another MSI board with a similar chip set, but not the same. He told me it
> was important because one "usually has to reformat the hard drive if the chip
> sets are different."

They are feeding you a load of crap.  The hard drive should not even be
accessed at the point where you are getting that message.

If there is a problem with ide drives being read differently, it is a
different message.  (Errors of sectors not ending on the correct
boundaries or some such.) 

Sounds like the cpu heat sensors got fried in a power surge.  Replacing
the motherboard is the only fix I know for that.  (Or you can run
without the sensors and hope that the fan fails open and not closed.)

> 
>    A bunch of you are hardware gurus so I'd like your opinion of both the
> statement that a different chip set could require a hard drive reformat
> (apparently this is more of a Microsoft issue, but even so ...) and whether
> the report of no CPU fan is a transient event about which I should not worry.
> Candidly, if I don't have to strip the box and swap system boards I'd prefer
> to save the time and money. However, if that's the prudent thing to do I'll
> do it this weekend.
> 
>    What sayith y'all?
> 
> Rich
>

Alan wrote:

> accessed at the point where you are getting that message.

They weren't saying it was... they were saying if you get a new
motherboard, you'd likely need to reformat.  This is mostly true for
most of their customers: Windows users.

> If there is a problem with ide drives being read differently, it is a
> different message.  (Errors of sectors not ending on the correct
> boundaries or some such.) 

Yeah, the hard drive wasn't the problem...

> Sounds like the cpu heat sensors got fried in a power surge.  Replacing
> the motherboard is the only fix I know for that.  (Or you can run
> without the sensors and hope that the fan fails open and not closed.)

Actually, no.  The BIOS spits out quite different errors when the CPU is
overheating (or seeming to overheat).  For years, motherboards have been
able to detect the speed of the fans spinning.  It seems either the CPU
fan was plugged in to a different plug on the motherboard (unlikely
considering Rich wasn't messing with that part of the system) or the fan
speed sensors were messed up.

Rob

From brent at servuhome.net  Fri Aug  5 20:42:47 2005

	<Pine.LNX.4.62.0508052025260.10736@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <42F431B7.1090609@servuhome.net>

If you didn't tell ENU that you were running Linux of some sort, they 
probably assumed it was a Windows system.
And in the case of Windows, sometimes it requires a reinstall, and in 
extreme cases, a reformat to get Windows to run properly with a new 
board/chipset.
This is due to the drivers, and hardware changes. Liken it to this, you 
stripped out all device modules out of your kernel, except the ones you 
have and are currently using. You then do a motherboard upgrade, one 
that has a totaly different chipset vendor.
Linux may, or may not accept the new hardware, based on what kernel 
modules you have enabled.
Albeit, most people just leave all the modules enabled, and are only 
used when loaded.

So, claiming the ENU employee on the other end of the line was full of 
crap, or otherwise trying to mislead you, that is the load of crap here.

Following this thread, just sounds like you got a flaky motherboard.
ENU has a very very good return policy, just pack it up like new, take 
it back, tell them the issue, and get a new board. Unless otherwise a 
BIOS update, or other tweaks fix this issue.

From athlonrob at axpr.net  Sat Aug  6 05:50:06 2005
From: athlonrob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Sat, 06 Aug 2005 05:50:06 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] [OT] Hardware Question
In-Reply-To: <1123310385.27221.21.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.44.0508052218560.2822-100000@www.fnordora.org>
	<1123310385.27221.21.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <42F4B1FE.3060206@axpr.net>

Vram wrote:
> On Fri, 2005-08-05 at 22:20 -0700, alan wrote:
>>I am used to those sensors being handled by the motherboard.  (The board 
>>drives the fans, not the power supply.)
> 
> Where does the board get the power???? If not the Power
> sUpply..<Microsoft???>

True, but the power supply isn't smart enough to feed the motherboard
varying amounts of power depending on feedback from the motherboard.  It
doesn't control the fans plugged in to the motherboard, the motherboard
does.

Now, if Rich's CPU fan plugs in to one of those D-plugs coming out of
the power supply, the power supply could theoretically control its
speed.  However, I've never heard of such technology.  CPU fans have,
IIRC, had the ability to self-throttle themselves for a few years... and
 motherboards could throttle the fans if they wanted to, as well.

Motherboards have been refusing to power up if a spinning fan isn't
plugged in to the CPU fan plug on the motherboard for years.  I fixed an
old KT133-based board a few years back when a BIOS update required the
CPU fan be plugged in to the motherboard rather than directly in to the
power supply via an adapter.

Rob

It's time again. This August 21 (yes, that's two weeks from Sunday), Free
Geek will again be hosting LPI Exams for those who wish to have their
Linux-guruhood certified by an independent organization. There will be
several spaces available for both 101 and 102 exams, so if you haven't
signed up yet, just let me know.

The Linux Professional Institute (lpi.org) is based in Canada and offers
their exams all over the world. They currently have two levels of
certification (Junior Level Administration, Intermediate Level
Administration), and are developing a third. There are two tests for each
level, and you can take any one of those four tests at Free Geek for $40
(significantly less than at "normal" testing centers). Part of your test fee
goes to help support Free Geek's programs. 
Everyone wins!

You can find a schedule of LPI exams at http://freegeek.org/lpi ,
information about the exams themselves and preparing for them at
http://lpi.org/en/lpic.html , and you can contact other local folks about
preparing for these exams at http://lists.freegeek.org/listinfo/lpi-study .
It's all lined up. All you have to do is sign up - and study!

Peace

As I go off to college, I want to build myself a reliable storage system
to backup data from my laptop. It seems like disk-to-disk backups are
the thing to do these days. I have 2 120GB drives and one 250GB drive
and a reasonble box to put them in.

I've done some googling and so I have some ideas how I'm going to set
this up, but I am open to suggestions if anyone has any to give. :-)

I found the Linux Software RAID Howto:

http://www.tldp.org/HOWTO/Software-RAID-HOWTO.html

After reading it and looking at my storage requirements, I think I will
use the 2 120's in a RAID 1 and use the 250 as a standalone drive for
that data that I don't care that much about.

This leads to a more specific question: in what way are my drives likely
to fail? Will they throw up a red flag saying "I'm dead, replace me", or
will they be less straightforward about it and start flipping bits in my
data? If the latter is at all likely, I think I need a third disk in the
RAID 1 -- is this true?

Thanks,

Evan

From aaron at bavariati.org  Sat Aug  6 21:28:46 2005
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Sat, 6 Aug 2005 21:28:46 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Reliable storage on a shoestring
In-Reply-To: <1123387918.5559.18.camel@luffy>
References: <1123387918.5559.18.camel@luffy>
Message-ID: <20050807042846.GJ9918@bavariati.org>

On Sat, Aug 06, 2005 at 09:11:58PM -0700, Evan Heidtmann wrote:
> This leads to a more specific question: in what way are my drives likely
> to fail? Will they throw up a red flag saying "I'm dead, replace me", or
> will they be less straightforward about it and start flipping bits in my
> data? 

Drives almost always fail two ways: slow decay (more and more unreadable
sectors) or catastrophic (won't do nuthin'.)  Either way, silent data
corruption doesn't happen.

With RAID, unreadable sectors aren't a big problem; the system bitches
about it in the syslog and gets the data from a good drive.  Same deal
with a dead drive.

Modern IDE drives have a thingy called SMART for monitoring drive
health.  You can install SMARTd, which will keep an eye on it.


Aaron, who doesn't miss MFM, RLL or ESDI *at* *all*.

References: <1123387918.5559.18.camel@luffy>
	<20050807042846.GJ9918@bavariati.org>
Message-ID: <f4468cae050807083428e09d93@mail.gmail.com>

If you use a very current OS (Fedora4 or RHEL/CentOS4), the md
(Software RAID) drivers are configured by default to alert you (via
syslog/logwatch at least, possibly mail to root I forget).  As Aaron
mentioned, use smartd which also comes standard with all modern
distros.

Just as a note, I have seen 'silent' corruption in a couple of cases,
where you don't know your files are toast until things stop working
right, start segfaulting and you MD5 them.  I've seen this during some
filesystem testing and tripped an XFS bug, and also some straight up
3Ware issues.

/eli



On 8/6/05, Aaron Burt <aaron at bavariati.org> wrote:
> On Sat, Aug 06, 2005 at 09:11:58PM -0700, Evan Heidtmann wrote:
> > This leads to a more specific question: in what way are my drives likely
> > to fail? Will they throw up a red flag saying "I'm dead, replace me", or
> > will they be less straightforward about it and start flipping bits in my
> > data?
> 
> Drives almost always fail two ways: slow decay (more and more unreadable
> sectors) or catastrophic (won't do nuthin'.)  Either way, silent data
> corruption doesn't happen.
> 
> With RAID, unreadable sectors aren't a big problem; the system bitches
> about it in the syslog and gets the data from a good drive.  Same deal
> with a dead drive.
> 
> Modern IDE drives have a thingy called SMART for monitoring drive
> health.  You can install SMARTd, which will keep an eye on it.
> 
> 
> Aaron, who doesn't miss MFM, RLL or ESDI *at* *all*.

From clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com  Sun Aug  7 14:11:40 2005
From: clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com (Evan Heidtmann)
Date: Sun, 07 Aug 2005 14:11:40 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Is there a debian installed packages backup script?
In-Reply-To: <1123438953.8392.13.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <1123438953.8392.13.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <1123449100.12605.7.camel@luffy>

On Sun, 2005-08-07 at 11:22 -0700, William Leake wrote:
> Hello,
> 
> I am not sure if this is worded right, but here goes.  Is there a
> utility that reads dpkg's (or apt's?) database to see what has been
> installed, and creates a backup script that will install all of those
> packages again? 

I don't know of a script that does that. But it should be pretty easy.
dpkg can give you a list of packages and what you wanted to do with
them. From dpkg(8):

       To make a local copy of the package selection states:
            dpkg --get-selections >myselections

       You might transfer this file to another computer, and install it
       there with:
            dpkg --set-selections <myselections
       Note that this will not actually install or remove anything, but
       just  set  the  selection  state on the requested packages.  You
       will need  some  other  application  to  actually  download  and
       install  the  requested  packages.  For example, run dselect and
       choose "Install".

       Ordinarily, you will find that dselect(8) provides a more conve-
       nient way to modify the package selection states.

dselect seems to be in disfavor these days. Maybe aptitude or even plain
apt-get will do.

Hope this helps,
Evan

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Sun Aug  7 15:22:38 2005
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Sun, 7 Aug 2005 16:22:38 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] Reliable storage on a shoestring
In-Reply-To: <1123452307.12605.21.camel@luffy>
References: <1123387918.5559.18.camel@luffy>
	<20050807042846.GJ9918@bavariati.org>
	<1123452307.12605.21.camel@luffy>
Message-ID: <f4468cae05080715222f6c281e@mail.gmail.com>

There are only two tricks to having a bootable SWRAID volume, provided
your distro handles creating and installing on the volumes properly:

1) Set your BIOS to boot from all physical drives in the array,
otherwise if one DOES fail, you won't be able to boot from the
secondary if it's not listed.

2) RH (and some other distros) don't install GRUB to the bootsect of
the drives properly... if your install goes fine, but you can't get
the OS to boot post back here (presuming you have another box ;)

You _could_ use your 250G in a RAID5 array with the two 120's but you
don't want to, firstly RAID5  treats all members of the array as the
smallest member size... you could have a 500G drive and four 100's and
only 100G will be utilized from the 500.  RAID5 will also only grant
you (N-1) * members in capacity, so you'd only get 240G from three
120G drives.

/eli

From fpikus at gmail.com  Sun Aug  7 15:41:32 2005
From: fpikus at gmail.com (Fedor Pikus)
Date: Sun, 7 Aug 2005 15:41:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Reliable storage on a shoestring
In-Reply-To: <f4468cae05080715222f6c281e@mail.gmail.com>
References: <1123387918.5559.18.camel@luffy>
	<20050807042846.GJ9918@bavariati.org>
	<1123452307.12605.21.camel@luffy>
	<f4468cae05080715222f6c281e@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <a4fbdc7b0508071541369547dc@mail.gmail.com>

On 8/7/05, Eli Stair <eli.stair at gmail.com> wrote:
> 2) RH (and some other distros) don't install GRUB to the bootsect of
> the drives properly... if your install goes fine, but you can't get
> the OS to boot post back here (presuming you have another box ;)

First of all, you cannot boot from software RAID-5, only from a mirror
RAID (RAID-1).
As far as GRUB, any distro which relies on grub-install won't be able
to install GRUB on soft-RAID. I don't think GRUB documentation
mentions how to do it either. But there is a way, you have to get to
the grub prompt and do it manually. Here is how to do it for a RAID-1
built from hda1 and hdb1 (# is shell prompt, grub> is GRUB prompt,
everything else is what GRUB prints out):

# grub
grub> root (hd0,0)
 Filesystem type is ext2fs, partition type 0xfd 
grub> setup (hd0) 
 Checking if "/boot/grub/stage1" exists... yes
 Checking if "/boot/grub/stage2" exists... yes
 Checking if "/boot/grub/e2fs_stage1_5" exists... yes
 Running "embed /boot/grub/e2fs_stage1_5 (hd0)"...  16 sectors are embedded.
succeeded
 Running "install /boot/grub/stage1 (hd0) (hd0)1+16 p
(hd0,0)/boot/grub/stage2 /boot/grub/grub.conf"... succeeded
Done.

grub> root (hd1,0)
 Filesystem type is ext2fs, partition type 0xfd

grub> setup (hd1) 
 Checking if "/boot/grub/stage1" exists... yes
 Checking if "/boot/grub/stage2" exists... yes
 Checking if "/boot/grub/e2fs_stage1_5" exists... yes
 Running "embed /boot/grub/e2fs_stage1_5 (hd1)"...  16 sectors are embedded.
succeeded
 Running "install /boot/grub/stage1 (hd1) (hd1)1+16 p
(hd1,0)/boot/grub/stage2 /boot/grub/grub.conf"... succeeded
Done.

grub> quit

If you look carefully, you're actually booting from either half of the
mirror. Your /etc/grub.conf should reflect this:

default=0
fallback=1
timeout=5
splashimage=(hd0,0)/boot/grub/splash.xpm.gz
hiddenmenu
title Fedora Core (2.6.12-1.1372_FC3)
        root (hd0,0)
        kernel /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.12-1.1372_FC3 ro root=/dev/md0
        initrd /boot/initrd-2.6.12-1.1372_FC3.img
title Fedora Core (2.6.12-1.1372_FC3) Mirror
        root (hd1,0)
        kernel /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.12-1.1372_FC3 ro root=/dev/md0
        initrd /boot/initrd-2.6.12-1.1372_FC3.img


-- 
Fedor G Pikus (fpikus at gmail.com)
http://www.pikus.net
http://wild-light.com

From jeme at brelin.net  Mon Aug  8 02:37:56 2005
From: jeme at brelin.net (Jeme A Brelin)

	<Pine.LNX.4.62.0508071210230.25872@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0508080221280.9157@shaft.bitmine.net>


On Sun, 7 Aug 2005, Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Sun, 7 Aug 2005, William Leake wrote:
>> I am not sure if this is worded right, but here goes.  Is there a 
>> utility that reads dpkg's (or apt's?) database to see what has been 
>> installed, and creates a backup script that will install all of those 
>> packages again? If this does not exist, does it sound like something 
>> that would be useful? Maybe it could be updated after each install, 
>> removal, or upgrade, or via a cron job after a certain interval. My 
>> thinking is that if you can just back-up the config files like /etc* 
>> and /home*, and the list of packages, it would be more efficient if you 
>> had to reinstall.
>
>  What does this buy you over restoring from a tape or external hard 
> drive?

It buys you a gig or two of backup space and it ensures that your restore 
is the latest and greatest pristine binaries from the Debian archive.

You don't need to keep a backup (or twenty) of the binaries that come in 
your distribution because they're already archived all over the net and 
you're just probably just going to rewrite them with a fresh update as 
soon as possible anyway.  With this approach, you keep local backups of 
local data (/etc, /home, /usr/local) and let the rest of the internet 
worry about backing up the data you just pulled off the internet in the 
first place.

This seems to me very much consistent with The Debian Way -- keep a 
reliable, semi-recent install disc around and restore your system from 
your apt archives and local backups as needed.  The scripts in the package 
archives will show you what changes need to be made to your old 
configuration files to make them compatible with the new versions of the 
packages and you then choose to let the files be automatically modified, 
modify them or not at your own convenience and let the install proceed, or 
abort the installation.

I have a cron job that dumps my dpkg selections to a file in /etc, 
William.  It's VERY trivial and straightforward and contains all the data 
I need to restore a new system.  I just use vi and some carefully 
constructed regex replacements to trim the list down to currently 
installed selections and restore those with something like

$ apt-get update       #  Read my new sources.list and get package data
$ apt-get dist-upgrade     #  Update installed packages from new archives
$ apt-get install `cat /etc/selections`  #  Restore packages from old list

This can be done from a very minimal install of pretty much any version of 
Debian.

> I've used a full backup on tape to restore what came off one drive onto 
> a new hard drive. I think this is what you want to do, correct?

Naw.  What if you find out you've been running some h4X0r'd /sbin/init? 
It could be that no backup you have would allow a clean upgrade without 
passing on its poisoned payload.

J.

In-Reply-To: <20050808093520.77D6243D0@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200508082203.j78M3aG9016508@m5p.com>

>From: Evan Heidtmann <clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com>
> On Sat, 2005-08-06 at 21:28 -0700, Aaron Burt wrote:
> > On Sat, Aug 06, 2005 at 09:11:58PM -0700, Evan Heidtmann wrote:
> > > This leads to a more specific question: in what way are my drives likely
> > > to fail? Will they throw up a red flag saying "I'm dead, replace me", or
> > > will they be less straightforward about it and start flipping bits in my
> > > data? 
> > 
> > Drives almost always fail two ways: slow decay (more and more unreadable
> > sectors) or catastrophic (won't do nuthin'.)  Either way, silent data
> > corruption doesn't happen.
> 
> That's very good to hear. It appears that old drives did silent
> corruption, but new drives don't. All my drives are relatively new, so I
> should be fine there.

No recent drive is ever supposed to fail by returning bad data, by recent
I'd say new within the past 10 years or more than say 100MB. Alan's is
not the only report I've seen of a drive giving back bad data when it was
dead though. So modern drives generally fail by failing to give back
data, but very rarely they fail by giving back garbage.

Manufacturers might claim it never happens, but Alan is reasonably sane
so I tend to believe his report, and I have seen other reports of it
happening (rarely, but they are out there if you look).

From Allen.Russell at kpchr.org  Mon Aug  8 15:15:48 2005
From: Allen.Russell at kpchr.org (Russell, Allen)
Date: Mon, 8 Aug 2005 15:15:48 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Reliable storage on a shoestring
Message-ID: <45D11DADA87D464BAE689E1DD5B6046047545E@merlin.chr.or.kp.org>

Recently I thought I had a case of "silent data corruption" on a disk drive.  However, what it really turned out to be was a case of intermittently failing memory.  The apparent result is the same but the cause and treatment are quite different.

http://www.memtest.org/  should be your friend.

Allen

(with autorepeat). It seems unrelated to display activity. The volume
decreases to an almost inaudible level when I reduce the CPU frequency
from 1700Mhz to 600Mhz.

Has anyone seen this before? Any suggestions for how to go about
eliminating it?

From mikeraz at patch.com  Mon Aug  8 19:00:08 2005
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Mon, 8 Aug 2005 19:00:08 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] A cheer for Linux - largefile
Message-ID: <20050809020008.GA29494@patch.com>

At work I'm dealing with Solaris 9 systems.  One of them is running an
application with some performance issues so the vendor asked for a debug
log file.  The first one created was 11G.  No problem, I thought, log
files compress really well.  gzip barfed on the file.  A little research
showed that not all programs in the Solaris world can work with large
files - in fact there's a man page entry that describes what is
"largefile" capable and what is not.  It indicated that compress would
be able to handle the file.  compress barfed.  As did split, perl and
every other utility I tried. They all died about the time the read hit
the 4GB mark.

So I got creative.  Re-ran the process, did a `tail -f logfile | perl
<prg2make_little_files>`  it barfed.  The vendor said to send what I had
and they'd work with that.

Come home tonight and create an 11G file by catting all the logfiles I
have on my system multiple times.  Everything I try on it works.
Increase the file size to 17G everything I've tried so far still works.
This is all on a vanilla install Debian system.

One hears a lot of discussion about Linux having features needed by
enterprise customers.  In the area of support for large files it seems
to be doing quite nicely compared to a "real" Unix.

Subject: [PLUG] Re: Is there a debian installed packages backup script?
In-Reply-To: <20050808093520.77D6243D0@ulke.nakedape.cc>
References: <20050808093520.77D6243D0@ulke.nakedape.cc>

> Jeme A Brelin wrote:
> 
> I have a cron job that dumps my dpkg selections to a file in /etc, 
> William.  It's VERY trivial and straightforward and contains all the data 
> I need to restore a new system.  I just use vi and some carefully 
> constructed regex replacements to trim the list down to currently 
> installed selections and restore those with something like
> 
> $ apt-get update       #  Read my new sources.list and get package data
> $ apt-get dist-upgrade     #  Update installed packages from new archives
> $ apt-get install `cat /etc/selections`  #  Restore packages from old list
> 
> This can be done from a very minimal install of pretty much any version of 
> Debian.
> 
> J.

Thanks Jeme, that will accomplish most of the functionality I am looking
for.  How do you read the dpkg selections, and what is the trimming
syntax?  I would appreciate it if you could either post the script(s)
here, or for security reasons send it directly to me.  Hopefully that
request is not contrary to mailing list protocol :)

william dot leake at gmail dot com

W. Reid Leake



From pm at nellump.ath.cx  Tue Aug  9 11:02:34 2005
From: pm at nellump.ath.cx (Paul Mullen)
Date: Tue, 9 Aug 2005 11:02:34 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Is there a debian installed packages backup script?
In-Reply-To: <1123607820.29756.9.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <20050808093520.77D6243D0@ulke.nakedape.cc>
	<1123607820.29756.9.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <20050809180234.GA28203@nellump.ath.cx>

On Tue, Aug 09, 2005 at 10:17:00AM -0700, William Leake wrote:
> 
> How do you read the dpkg selections, and what is the trimming
> syntax?

The following one-liner works for me:

   dpkg -l | awk '/^ii/ {print $2}'

You could add something like this to your crontab:

   20 4 * * 0 dpkg -l | awk '/^ii/ {print $2}' > /etc/selections

That would run the selection-dumping "scriptette" every Sunday in the
wee hours of the morning.


Paul

  test-TO-IPCop.ovpn

 #OpenVPN Server conf

--cert. I tried pointing --cert to test.p12, then it complains about

On Tue, 9 Aug 2005, James H. Webb wrote:

> I need some help here.  My linux box ahs been having some problems with
> heat. My CPU fan was going bad. I switched fans today, put new conductor
> material on the CPU, placed the new fan on. Initially, I smelled something
> like a burning smell and there was no POST sounds. I redid the fan on the
> CPU and I still get no POST sounds. I've disconnected things and
> reconnected, but nothing.

Jim,

   Are you getting power to the system?

   Is there any sign of life when you press the power button?

   Last week I had a power supply fail. Today, the replacement power supply
failed. So I took that back and exchanged it for another one. Less than an
hour latter, that quit, too. After the unit sat for about 15 minutes it
powered on, but the BIOS monitor shows the temperatures as a bit high. Right
now I have both side panels off and a small floor fan blowing directly into
the case. It's stayed up for 2.5 hours now.

   In my case, I suspect that when the original power supply failed it
weakened the motherboard heat sensors/controllers. I'm having a more
experienced hardware mavin come by tomorrow morning. In the meantime, I'm
back to ENU for a replacement motherboard (an ASUS this time instead of
another MSI). I hope the chipset is supported by lm_sensor so I can build a
userland temperature/fan speed monitor and keep an eye on these.

   If your power supply is good, the CPU may have been terminally injured. Can
you get into the BIOS at all to see what the PC Health screen has to offer?

Rich

> Quick question.  I have a slightly newer box running another OS, which I
> won't mention, but do you think that I could just take the HD from this
> other box and toss it in there? Will I have much trouble getting the system
> to run. The HD has RH 8 on it? I have some important stuff on it.

Jim,

   If you put the new hard drive in the other box it should boot without a
problem. The OS, boot loader, and files are on the hard drive. It's only if
you try to run the X Window System that you may find the monitor settings
incompatible. In console mode it should boot and run just fine.

> It appears that the old CPU is an AMD Athlon 1.1 GHz.  When I smell it, it
> smells like something has burned up.

   Well, that's not a Good Thing. I suppose it's appropriate to write that the
old CPU is toast, eh?

   I don't mean to make light of the problem. They're never fun and they take
time away from more important things, such as billable work and marketing.

Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0508091825390.2711@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   I've googled for this board model and find mixed reviews: linuxhardware.org

   My MSI board (that I think is slightly injured, but nowhere near as bad as

to use the trackball, and the trackball is not smooth or easy to control in

   So, if I need a new motherboard, should I stay with the Asus or get another
MSI? The CPU is an Athlon XP+ 2200.

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Tue Aug  9 18:36:48 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Tue, 9 Aug 2005 18:36:48 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Dead Linux box
In-Reply-To: <NAEMLOHDCGGNKGMHCPFGIEALCDAA.jimwebb@charter.net>
References: <NAEMLOHDCGGNKGMHCPFGIEALCDAA.jimwebb@charter.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0508091831120.2711@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Tue, 9 Aug 2005, James H. Webb wrote:

> How would I go about changing monitor settings?  I can't remember since my
> linux box hasn't given me any trouble since I build it.

Jim,

   Depending on the distribution and version of X (i.e., XFree86 or X.org),
there's an Xconfigurator (or xconfigurator) somewhere in the system. Well,
there isn't on my Slackware-10.1 boxes.

   In /etc/X11 you'll find either XF86config (or XF86config4) or xorg.conf.
Your monitor manual will have the refresh frequencies you need. If you no
longer have the manual, google for that brand and model and you'll probably
get the data you need from the web.

   Also, before swapping drives, check your distribution (RH 8, I think you
wrote) for docs on how to configure X. Just to make sure.

Rich

-- 
Dr. Richard B. Shepard, President     |  Author of "Quantifying Environmental
Applied Ecosystem Services, Inc. (TM) |  Impact Assessments Using Fuzzy Logic"
<http://www.appl-ecosys.com>   Voice: 503-667-4517   Fax: 503-667-8863


From athlonrob at axpr.net  Tue Aug  9 19:16:08 2005
From: athlonrob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Tue, 09 Aug 2005 19:16:08 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Any Experience With Asus A7N8X A+L Motherboard?
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0508091825390.2711@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0508091825390.2711@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <42F96368.70306@axpr.net>

Rich Shepard wrote:
>   I've googled for this board model and find mixed reviews:
> linuxhardware.org
> likes it; others find it won't boot linux. Some complain that the nVidia
> chip
> set is difficult to set up because the drivers are proprietary.

I'm not familiar with that particular A7N8X variety, but have used a few
others in the past.  If it's one of the dual-channel-capable
varieties... I'd go for it.  They're very fast with more memory
bandwidth than the CPU knows what to do with.

As far as drivers being proprietary - if you use a recent kernel, it's
all supported out-of-the-box.  ALSA drivers support the sound via the
i8x0 drivers, networking is supported by the ForcedEth driver, and
everything else pretty much just works.  I think there is IDE controller
support specific to the nForce in the kernel these days now, as well.

I've loved all my nForce-based motherboards I've had, including the
A7N8X varieties...

Rob

Rob,

   Yes, it is a dual-channel-capable board; up to 800MHz memory bandwidth, if
I recall correctly.

> As far as drivers being proprietary - if you use a recent kernel, it's all
> supported out-of-the-box. ALSA drivers support the sound via the i8x0
> drivers, networking is supported by the ForcedEth driver, and everything
> else pretty much just works. I think there is IDE controller support
> specific to the nForce in the kernel these days now, as well.

   I will find out, then, if the 2.4.29 kernel is sufficiently recent. :-)

> I've loved all my nForce-based motherboards I've had, including the A7N8X

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Aug 10 07:00:30 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 10 Aug 2005 07:00:30 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Using lm_sensors with 2.4.x kernel
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0508100643170.1574@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   Anyone here with experience in installing, configuring, and using
lm_sensors?

   I know that my motherboard (VIA KT333 chipset) has temperature, fan speed,
and voltage sensors because the BIOS provides this information in the "PC
Health Status" screen. However, that's not practical for monitoring a
running/working system.

   Enter i2c and lm_sensors.

   When I grep for i2c in /proc/* on my Slackware-10.1 system with the 2.4.29
kernel I learn that it's present in kcore. Therefore, I assume it's been
installed as part of the stock kernel. I found a Slackware package for
lm_sensors-2.9.1 and installed that (since i2c is supposedly already
available). Not finding any man page or docs for lm_sensors, I asked google
for help and it returned a very useful page:
                  <http://xlife.zuavra.net/columns/20020807/>
which I've read and have been trying to implement.

   According to this page, I should be able to run 'sensors-detect' to
configure them. When I try to run this command I learn this:

[root at salmo ~]# sensors-detect
No i2c device files found. Use prog/mkdev/mkdev.sh to create them.

   I cannot discern how to run mkdev/mkdev.sh so it works:

[root at salmo ~]# prog/mkdev/mkdev.sh
-su: prog/mkdev/mkdev.sh: No such file or directory

   Same results if I try just 'mkdev/mkdev.sh'.

   Trying 'sensors -s' results in this:

[root at salmo ~]# sensors -s
Can't access procfs/sysfs file
Unable to find i2c bus information;
For 2.6 kernels, make sure you have mounted sysfs and done
'modprobe i2c_sensor'!
For older kernels, make sure you have done 'modprobe i2c-proc'!
[root at salmo ~]# modprobe i2c-proc
[root at salmo ~]# sensors -s
No sensors found!

   So, I'm stuck, and I'd appreciate some thoughts from those of you who have
successfully installed this userland tool.

TIA,

Rich

Dr. Richard B. Shepard, President     |  Author of "Quantifying Environmental
Applied Ecosystem Services, Inc. (TM) |  Impact Assessments Using Fuzzy Logic"
<http://www.appl-ecosys.com>   Voice: 503-667-4517   Fax: 503-667-8863


From raanders at acm.org  Wed Aug 10 07:14:40 2005
From: raanders at acm.org (Roderick A. Anderson)
Date: Wed, 10 Aug 2005 07:14:40 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Using lm_sensors with 2.4.x kernel
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0508100643170.1574@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.33.0508100707060.14747-100000@main.cyber-office.net>

On Wed, 10 Aug 2005, Rich Shepard wrote:

>    Anyone here with experience in installing, configuring, and using
> lm_sensors?

Sorry no but I have some ideas on your later problems.

<snip />

>    I cannot discern how to run mkdev/mkdev.sh so it works:
> 
> [root at salmo ~]# prog/mkdev/mkdev.sh
> -su: prog/mkdev/mkdev.sh: No such file or directory

Rich,

Try using find looking for 'mkdev.sh'.  I may be hidden elsewhere.  Also 
consider using -iname.

On SysV-ish ( verses BSD-ish ) systems the command is MAKEDEV and the 
script is located in /dev.  Look in the script and you will probably find 
it accepts several commandline switches or arguments.  Look for the 
lm_sensors one.

>    Same results if I try just 'mkdev/mkdev.sh'.

See above re: commandline switches.


Good luck,
Rod

    "Open Source Software - You usually get more than you pay for..."
     "Build A Brighter Lamp :: Linux Apache {middleware} PostgreSQL"

I'm looking to do some advanced routing on Linux box.  I've found the 
"Adv-Routing-HOWTO" on The Linux Documentation Project but it is dated 
July of 2002.  Things get old fast on the Internet so does anyone know of 
later docs/pubs/books on, advanced, routing?

FYI, this is for a Linux-Vserver with three NICs: one for the LAN and the 
other two going to two different routers ( ISPs ) to the Internet.  Each 
guest server may access the LAN and one of the WAN interfaces.  ( This is 
not your normal -- well my research says so -- networking issue.  And the 
Linux-Vserver kernel adds a few interesting twists.  :-)


Thanks,
Rod

ok its possible that ur boot directory has changed
boot linux in rescue mode
i.e bot it from CD & type

linux rescue
at boot prompt after that check in /etc/grub.conf file & check wheather it
points to correct root partition

if u dont know abt where ur root partition is :
then give this command at the cmd-prompt

#fdisk -l

I also saw this: root=LABEL=/
to
root=/dev/hda*

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Aug 10 10:57:05 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 10 Aug 2005 10:57:05 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] FW: Can't bootup linux
In-Reply-To: <NAEMLOHDCGGNKGMHCPFGCEBDCDAA.jimwebb@charter.net>
References: <NAEMLOHDCGGNKGMHCPFGCEBDCDAA.jimwebb@charter.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0508101053260.2849@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Wed, 10 Aug 2005, James H. Webb wrote:

> I googled and got the following information; and Where is the correct root
> partition in RH8?

Jim,

   The distribution doesn't matter. Most of the time folks put the / in
/dev/hda1; the first partition of the first hard drive.

   It doesn't help you at this stage, but I found that Red Hat's partition
labels were more of a hassle than a help to me.

   If you put in a Knoppix disk and (re)boot the system it will come up and
let you click on the image of /dev/hda. Then you can confirm that /dev/hda1
is the root partition and you can pass that to grub at boot time from your
hard drive.

   Sorry you're having such a hard time with that system.

Rich

From sean.whitney at gmail.com  Wed Aug 10 14:20:58 2005
From: sean.whitney at gmail.com (Sean Whitney)
Date: Wed, 10 Aug 2005 14:20:58 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Is there a debian installed packages backup script?
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0508080221280.9157@shaft.bitmine.net>
References: <1123438953.8392.13.camel@localhost.localdomain>
	<Pine.LNX.4.62.0508071210230.25872@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.61.0508080221280.9157@shaft.bitmine.net>
Message-ID: <1123708859.5010.2.camel@einstein.seansdomain.org>

dpkg --get-selections > /etc/selections

this and a copy /etc/ is all that I need to rebuild a box.

install a core os, replace /etc/ with the backup,

dpkg --set-selections < /etc/selections

apt-get dselect-upgrade

voila, done.....

Sean



On Mon, 2005-08-08 at 02:37 -0700, Jeme A Brelin wrote:
> I have a cron job that dumps my dpkg selections to a file in /etc, 
> William.  It's VERY trivial and straightforward and contains all the
> data 
> I need to restore a new system.  I just use vi and some carefully 
> constructed regex replacements to trim the list down to currently 
> installed selections and restore those with something like
> 
> $ apt-get update       #  Read my new sources.list and get package
> data
> $ apt-get dist-upgrade     #  Update installed packages from new
> archives
> $ apt-get install `cat /etc/selections`  #  Restore packages from old
> list

From nickaubert at gmail.com  Wed Aug 10 14:34:56 2005
From: nickaubert at gmail.com (Nick Aubert)
Date: Wed, 10 Aug 2005 14:34:56 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Quiet intermittent beeping on Thinkpad T42
In-Reply-To: <1123631650.3062.4.camel@luffy>
References: <1123550597.20766.27.camel@luffy>
	<c1a525c505080912515679a5a@mail.gmail.com>
	<1123631650.3062.4.camel@luffy>
Message-ID: <55ef240d050810143455ba5c1b@mail.gmail.com>

Try adjusting the volume in alsamixer.  In gentoo, alsamixer comes
with the alsa-utils ebuild.  If you're not running gentoo, it may be
part of a similarly named package, or may already be installed.  There
are also exist gui-frontend projects for alsa.  Since the problem only
manifests itself in Linux, it may be alsa-related.  I'd start looking
at your alsa configuration.  If you aren't running alsa, then maybe
you should.

If that doesn't work, you did suggest sort of a solution yourself:
> The sound disappears when any constant CPU activity occurs: playing an
> OGG (muted)

Not very elegant, but less cpu-intensive than no-hlt to the kernel. 
I'd suggest you play thinkpad-beeping.ogg on an endless loop, for
sheer irony.

-Nick

Ah, I got it working.  It was a matter of enabling
CONFIG_SCSI_MULTI_LUN.  Also, in order to detect the CF, it required
having some SmartMedia in the device as well as the CF.  Good enough
for now.

> > Try using find looking for 'mkdev.sh'.  I may be hidden elsewhere.  Also
> > consider using -iname.
> 
> Rod,
> 
>    I'm sorry that I forgot to mention that I used find for mkdev.sh and i2c.
> No luck on either.
> 
> > On SysV-ish ( verses BSD-ish ) systems the command is MAKEDEV and the
> > script is located in /dev. Look in the script and you will probably find it
> > accepts several commandline switches or arguments. Look for the lm_sensors
> > one.
> 
>    Aha! There it is. But, it does not know how to make 'makdev.sh'. So, I keep
> searching.
> 
> Many thanks,
> 
> Rich

Rich,

I looked at the startup script.  It tries to load the i2c module, if
the /sys/bus/i2c file exists.  So I loaded the module by:
/sbin/modprobe i2c-dev 

It loaded fine on my Fedora FC3 distribution.  Kernel 2.6.12-1.1372_FC3.

I then ran sensors-detect. This creates the /etc/sysconfig/lm_sensors
file or I should say it configures the file with the necessary modules
to load.

HTH,
Ken of CAD 2 CAM

From johnxj at comcast.net  Wed Aug 10 21:57:15 2005
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jordan)
Date: Wed, 10 Aug 2005 21:57:15 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] ipods
In-Reply-To: <1123734921.6861.27.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <42FA783B.6195.202EEE90@localhost>

On 10 Aug 2005, at 21:35, Dirk Ouellette wrote:

> Does anyone have experience with ipods and linux?
> Thanks

I have some experience. Mostly trial and error. Wait ... make that 
mostly error. No, wait ... make that just about 100% error.

The main utility you need is gtkpod. This will allow you to transfer 
your mp3s to the iPod. Unfortunately, I found it hopelessly 
unintuitive and there are no help files. Also, be sure to unmount the 
iPod before disconnecting it, as there are reports that failing to do 
so will wipe out the iPod database.

You also need something to create mp3s. The iPod cannot take 
any format but mp3 -- no ogg, flac, wav, etc. Just mp3. As a result, 
if you point gtkod at a file that is not an mp3, the file will not even 
appear in gtkpod's list of files to install on the iPod.

Ripping CDs to mp3s is another problem area. Most say the best 
is Grip, and that may be true. However, I couldn't figure it out. 
There are a bazillion options with little explanation of what they do. 
I finally gave up and found some freeware and shareware rippers to 
put on my Windows box. But they turned out to be so bad that I 
had to give up on that route as well. I recently found that k3b can 
rip CDs, and I did do one CD with it. However, the quality was poor. 
Hopefully there is an option somewhere for greater quality, but I've 
been too busy to go back and try it again.

K3b has another minor problem for me, as I am using Gnome on 
Ubuntu-64, 5.04. It installed, but there is no listing to click on in the 
Applications drop-down list. I have to launch it manually.


From pm at nellump.ath.cx  Thu Aug 11 00:16:26 2005
From: pm at nellump.ath.cx (Paul Mullen)
Date: Thu, 11 Aug 2005 00:16:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] CD Ripping Apps  WAS: ipods
Message-ID: <20050811071626.GA22556@nellump.ath.cx>

On Wed, Aug 10, 2005 at 09:57:15PM -0700, John Jordan wrote:
> 
> Ripping CDs to mp3s is another problem area. Most say the best is
> Grip, and that may be true. However, I couldn't figure it out.
---8<-----
> I recently found that k3b can rip CDs, and I did do one CD with it.
> However, the quality was poor.  Hopefully there is an option
> somewhere for greater quality, but I've been too busy to go back and
> try it again.

I'm a big fan of abcde ("A Better CD Encoder"), a shell script that
reduces the process of a ripping an audio CD to MP3 or Vorbis to a
nearly trivial affair. It's text mode, but that shouldn't scare anyone
away.  Pop a CD in, "abcde", answer a few questions, check over the
album info retrieved from CDDB, and let it rip.

<http://lly.org/~rcw/abcde/page/>
<http://www.hispalinux.es/~data/abcde.php>


Paul

References: <20050811071626.GA22556@nellump.ath.cx>
Message-ID: <1123773683.7390.6.camel@einstein.seansdomain.org>

I really like abcde as well, I wrote a wrapper around it that checks a
cdrom drive status.  If it finds something there is starts
ripping/encoding automatically.

Works great!!!!!

I have this on a headless box, and it starts on boot.  Pop in a CD and
watch it go....

Sean

=================================
#!/bin/sh
CDROM=/proc/ide/hdg
DELAY=10
RIP="/usr/bin/abcde -x -b -N"

while true; do
     sleep $DELAY
     CAPACITY=`cat $CDROM/capacity`
     if [ $CAPACITY -gt 0 ]; then
         $RIP
     fi
done
=================================

> nearly trivial affair. It's text mode, but that shouldn't scare anyone
> away.  Pop a CD in, "abcde", answer a few questions, check over the
> album info retrieved from CDDB, and let it rip.

***O'Reilly Connection Launched at OSCON
O'Reilly Connection is a tech-centric jobs and networking site for
developers and those who want to hire them. The service was conceived
and created by Greenplum, a company commercializing the open-source
database PostgreSQL for Business Intelligence.

On O'Reilly Connection, developers build a professional profile that
lays out their skills, and experience, and network in one place. They can
connect with peers and designate "go to" people-other site members whom
they consider experts in a particular technology area-and keep tabs on
those alpha geeks through personalized watch lists.

Employers can quickly search through O'Reilly Connection to find job or
consultant candidates with the specific qualifications they need.
Because profiles on O'Reilly Connection display members' networks, they
provide a richer picture than a standard resume. During the beta period,
O'Reilly Connection is offering free job postings to all members.
http://connection.oreilly.com/

***Give Your Business Logic a Framework with Drools 
 It's almost too easy to express your business logic as a spaghetti-code
fiasco. The result is hard to test, hard to maintain, and hard to
update. Rule engines offer an alternative: express your business logic
as rules, outside of your Java code, in a format even the business side
of the office can understand. Paul Browne uses the open source Drools
framework to introduce the idea.
http://www.onjava.com/pub/a/onjava/2005/08/03/drools.html

***Digital Photography Hack: A Hands-Free Shooting Rig 
Here's how to build a hands-free photography rig using an iSight, a
Bluetooth headset, a backpack, and a dash of AppleScript that enables
you to capture images on the go by simply speaking, "Take shot." Romain
Guy shows you how to build it.
http://www.macdevcenter.com/pub/a/mac/2005/08/05/photography.html

***Hacking Ebook Readers
The Sony Librie is a stunning, e-ink eBook reading device with the most
print-like book reading experience you can have at this time (the
display moves microscopic black and white particles held within
spherical microcapsules). For the most part, it hasn't been a success in
the market--Sony crippled it with DRM and only released in it Japan.
But the hackers and tinkerers of the world have taken this device and
modded the firmware. Now we're going to show you how to make non-DRMed
eBooks for free
http://www.makezine.com/blog/archive/2005/08/how_to_make_drm_1.html

From jeme at brelin.net  Thu Aug 11 14:45:45 2005

On Thu, 11 Aug 2005, Dirk Ouellette wrote:
> Thanks all for the ipod info. I'll look at gtkpod. I use grip and k3b 
> successfully with FC3.

I use Debian GNU/Linux exclusively at home and it plays nicely with my 
iPod.

I use gtkpod, too, but it has a handful of small issues (not nearly as 
many as iTunes, though -- phew, don't get me started).  Mostly those 
issues have to do with speed and updating lists of files.

The biggest trouble I had was making sure the firewire always worked.  I 
ended up switching to udev which creates a /dev/ipod when and only when my 
iPod is attached to my computer.  It also automounts the thing and gtkpod 
just works at that point.

I've formatted my iPod to vfat because I use it occassionally as a 
portable storage device and vfat is more widely supported than HFS.  I've 
not found a way to reformat it properly and safely except using the 
non-free software from Apple which runs only on OSX and Winders.

I've yet to put Linux on my iPod, but that may be in the future since that 
opens up the recording capabilities and allows playing more diverse file 
formats.

keywords:  2.6 udev hotplug gtkpod

J.

Most board vendors use pretty standard chips to monitor sensor outputs. 
Winbond being one of the most popular.
If lm_sensors has trouble reading the Winbond chip, its probably due to 
lm_sensors code being out of date, than being MSI's problem.

I've used lm_sensors in the past, with some pretty obscure boards, and 
always had success, either by messing with it for hours, or it would 
succesfuly read the output with no hackery.

And expecting some random MSI tech to support Linux is expecting 
Microsoft to volutarily open source their entire product line, total 
pipe dream.


From jeme at brelin.net  Thu Aug 11 18:14:09 2005
From: jeme at brelin.net (Jeme A Brelin)
Date: Thu, 11 Aug 2005 18:14:09 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] ipods
In-Reply-To: <42FB7F8E.15885.24338C10@localhost>
References: <42FA783B.6195.202EEE90@localhost>
	<42FB7F8E.15885.24338C10@localhost>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0508111740100.9157@shaft.bitmine.net>


On Thu, 11 Aug 2005, John Jordan wrote:
> I am trying to rip symphonies, and I find the sound really sucks unless 
> I rip at considerably more than 128 kbps.

That's perfectly understandable but depends somewhat on your ear.  I know 
people that can't listen to ANY music MP3s at less than 192kbps.  That's 
probably a good minimal starting point for music you care about.  Most of 
the stuff we find on the net these days ripped by those who care about 
sound quality is 320kbps.  Makes for big files, though.  192kbps is fine 
for me and my ear.

> Moreover, the default settings on Grip were to ogg, not mp3, and it took 
> a lot of figuring out in order to get it to rip to mp3s.

Um, that's weird.  I had lame and oggenc both installed and my version of 
grip (from the rarewarez Debian archive, I believe) had good default 
settings when I chose "lame" as my encoder in the "encode" configuration 
tab.

You'll further find that the "Options" tab under the "Encode" settings 
includes the bitrate.  Simple to change right there.

> Eventually I got it to rip, but it still insisted on ripping each track 
> as a separate "song." I needed to get it to rip each track as part of 
> one larger file for the whole symphony. In addition, I needed to add a 
> couple seconds quiet space in between each track. I never figured out 
> how to do that or how to get it to make several tracks into one file.

I don't know of anything out there that's going to do what you want. 
Everything I know that rips CDs rips them track by track.  You can, 
however, join the files when you're done.  You can even keep a couple of 
seconds of blank audio in a file and insert that between tracks (there 
may be a more elegant way of doing this, but I've spent no time thinking 
about it).

Quite frankly, however, I'm not sure why you'd do this.  The tracks are 
there to help you navigate the piece.  You lose nothing by keeping them 
except when you use "Shuffle" mode, but nobody does that with symphonic 
music anyway.  If you keep the tracks separate and make sure they have 
appropriate track numbers in their ID3 tags, you can listen to them in 
sequence (with appropriate between-track pauses) whenever you like.

> I also never figured out how to name the file and the tracks so they 
> would show up correctly on the iPod.

Naming them doesn't do diddly.  You need to modify their ID3 tags.  I use 
a combination of the id3v2 and mp3info utilities.  While the latter 
doesn't support version 2 tags, the former has crappy syntax and can be a 
pain to put into scripts.  Essentially, I use id3v2 for writing tags and 
mp3info for reading them in little shell scripts that I write to 
manipulate these things.

> After hours and hours of reading about it, perusing the help, scouring 
> the internet, and asking users on web pages and blogs, I just gave up on 
> it.

It's really no big deal.  What exactly is your problem?

> Not sure I really want iTunes on my Linux computer.

I wouldn't touch it with a ten meter cattle prod.

> As for Amarok, I just installed it, but there is no way to use it to 
> transfer files to an iPod. It seems to be strictly for burning CDs of 
> mp3s that have already been ripped.

I don't use KDE or any QT apps, but nothing in my reading indicates that 
Amarok is anything but a media player.

> I just got home after my last final exam (yay!!!) and tried k3b again. I

> kpps?) it is stuck at that and there is no user option to change it.

Again, grip is probably your tool.  It gives full access to the 
configurations of the backend utilities it calls and has a relatively 
straightforward interface.
