From ckonstan at lunarlogic.com  Sat Aug 20 11:39:00 2005
From: ckonstan at lunarlogic.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sat, 20 Aug 2005 11:39:00 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Any lispers out there?  Newbie needs booster shot
In-Reply-To: <4306DCED.9070403@cesmail.net>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0508181745160.6330@localhost>
	<86hddltp8q.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0508191746280.6355@localhost>
	<4306D36A.7050303@comcast.net> <4306DCED.9070403@cesmail.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0508201051320.6456@localhost>

Isn't AutoCAD also written in lisp?

I do make $$$ writing java struts apps.  I don't intend to quit my day
job.  But I am of the opinion that java sucks.  I think lisp compares
unfavorably to languages like java in today's business world for the
same reason all other business decisions are made - marketing.  Java
sounds cool, while lisp does not.  There is money behind java, pushing
it into our workplace, while lisp is nothing more than a specification
and a handful of kooks who keep it alive with their efforts in
implementing the specification.  God, I love those kooks!

Java is a one-dimensional language.  There is no precompiler and no
macros.  The code you write gets compiled at compile-time and run at
run-time, that's it.

When you add macros to the equation, you get another dimension that
complicates the understanding of how the language compiles and runs,
but provides a powerful means to abstract code.  Abstracting in java
is very different than abstracting in lisp.  I find the lisp way much
easier to develop.  You write the code in a very straightforward
style, and then you write a macro to generate that straightforward
code with as little input as possible.  The macro will then be your
copy-and-paste agent.  Simple but effective, it's amazing how well
macros can shorten and simplify code.  In java, you have to think
abstraction every step of the way, and you have to invent the
abstraction yourself, ending up with large, overblown systems of code
to do simple things.  If you really take java abstraction to the
limit, you often end up with reflection, which is klunky as heck and
difficult to read and debug.

C is better.  At least it does what you tell it to do, and it gives
you the power to do what you need.  You can have an 8-bit unsigned
value in C.  You can shift all 8 bits one place to the left.  Try that
in java.  It's even possible to implement the equivalent of a lisp
multimethod in C, using structs.  Java doesn't even support multile
inheritance.  There are implementations of lisp that get parsed and
rewritten as C, and then compiled.  C doesn't suck like java does.
But it still has certain shortcomings that are inherent in any
language with a rigid syntax that distinguishes between functions and
data.

The main area where lisp reigns supremee is its avoidance of strings.
The best example I can think of is the code I'm currently working on -
HTML generation.  Instead of writing a 50 megabyte library to separate
view and controller (I speak of struts/jsp here), a lisp library like
araneida can allow the HTML and the controller code to be freely
intermixed with no distincion between the two, and it requires only
one small lisp function to implement it.  Araneida's implementation of
an HTML library is very succinct; it does not actually implement
functions for HTML, BODY, etc.  It merely rewrites the list you feed
it as markup, and it doesn't care at all what the symbols mean.  A
library that does implement every HTML tag as a lisp function would
give the added advantage of telling you when you spell "html" wrong.
I think portable aserve and lml do exactly that.  Compile-time
checking of HTML - what a concept!  It is only possible when you don't
treat data and functions as different animals.

A fine example of string abuse is XML.  What is XML?  It is a bunch of
strings with metadata included to help you find the strings.  Struts
is built upon XML.  Can the compiler tell you when you made a typo in
your struts-config.xml file?  I should say not.  So how do you find
out you made a typo?  When you're starting at a blank white screen in
a browser, with no error message of any kind, of course.  XML is
derived from lisp s-expression syntax, but it took a seriously wrong
turn when it stringified the data.

A traditional java servlet puts all the HTML into strings.  Struts/jsp
gets you away from that, but at the price of introducing more huge XML
files into the equation, separation of the code into more files, and
poorer debugging.  And is it really doing anything different than a
lisp macro?  No - the jsp gets parsed and rewritten as a traditional
servlet.  Ironically, that is the smartest thing about using java to
write a webapp.  Of course, it's done in the usual, massively overbown
java style.

They say that the average programmer writes the same number of lines
of code in a day of work, no matter what language they are writing in.
Java, XML and XSL are highly verbose, while lisp is super compact.  I
think I heard somewhere that lisp weighs in at 1/10 the verbosity of
java.

So does the discriminating developer need to use sucky languages like
java 40 hours a week?  Because of human nature and marketing, the
answer is a somber "yes".  But there's no way I'm going to write
personal code in anything but common lisp from now on.

Carlos

On Sat, 20 Aug 2005, M. Edward (Ed) Borasky wrote:

> Date: Sat, 20 Aug 2005 00:34:05 -0700

> Renegade Penguin wrote:
>
>>  I have to speak up here.
>>
>>  LISP was a language we used in college, mostly for proof-of-concept.  The
>>  neat thing about LISP is that you can "black box" everything.  If you need
>>  a routine but don't know how to do that routine, you flowchart it as a
>>  black box, and then see about getting it to run or better yet get someone
>>  else to build it.
>>
>>  Eventually we discovered that LISP is more of a theoretical language.  Its
>>  compiled binaries are slow and clunky.
>>
>>  We determined that LISP stood for "Lots of Interesting Silly Parentheses."
>>
>>  If you're using JAVA, php, perl, C(+...), etc., STICK WITH THEM.  They
>>  will make you money.  LISP is more of an academic language for theories
>>  and such.
>
> I have to speak up here. Yes, Lisp is indeed a theoretical language. However, 
> its compiled binaries aren't necessarily slow and clunky any more. GCL, CMUCL 
> and SBCL, all free and open source, by the way, are quite efficient, and if 
> you want an even more efficient way, there's Scheme.
>
> There are two application areas where Lisp still reigns supreme. One is 
> symbolic mathematics -- Maxima is the best known free package. The other is 
> "artificial intelligence".
>
>>
>>  RP
>>

>> >  quotes.  Maybe I could use PRINC to strip the quotes, but that seems
>> >  hacky.  I really want to use the right method, and do it the Lisp Way.

>> >  possible.  I do need to deal with handler-class-name as a string when

# from Keith Lofstrom
# on Tuesday 16 August 2005 09:50 pm:

>Is there any easy-to-use graphics software library with simple
>routines that can be pointed at a two-level black-and-white
>png/jpg/gif/tif/other? bitmap image, then returns an array of
>ones and zeros? ?C, C++, or Perl would be acceptable.

Ok, too late.  Sorry.

The ImageMagick library is great.  You can load pretty much any format, 
reduce it to grayscale or B&W, save in almost any format, etc.

>The source data is unreadable ("GDS format" complex polygons)

I would be interested in adding support for this to the uber-converter 
or CAD::Drawing if you want to repeat or automate this process and have 
a decent spec.

In-Reply-To: <20050821172851.GS9531@merlot.com>

Message-ID: <4308CEB2.6020902@axpr.net>

Kurt Sussman wrote:
> I just put a Seagate 5400 RPM 80G drive in my notebook, and it's like a
> new computer. OOo starts in 12 seconds; it used to take almost 30.

I've considered getting a bigger/faster hard drive for my laptop
(Inspiron 2650) so it can last until Apple has x86 iBooks (I want to
upgrade to an iBook, but don't want to obsolete myself *just that fast*).

However, heat is a significant issue with my laptop.  I've fried one
wifi card (literally).  I'd rather not do more damage to the system than
is necessary if it is to last another 24-36 months.

What kind of heat output are you getting with said 5400RPM drive?  Is it
noticably warmer?  Does it suck down the battery faster?  In your speed
comparisons, what kind of drive was your old drive?

On my system, the small amount of RAM (384MB) and slow hard drive are
the two significant bottlenecks, both feeding off each other (reduced
disk caching due to small amount of RAM and slow swapping due to a slow
hard drive)... I can only upgrade my RAM to 512MB, leaving me with only
a faster hard drive to speed things up and last me another few years.

Rob

Subject: [PLUG] 2.5-inch drive (for laptop) recommendations?
In-Reply-To: <4308CEB2.6020902@axpr.net>
References: <86zmrdq8mp.fsf@coulee.tdb.com> <20050821172851.GS9531@merlot.com>

AthlonRob (athlonrob at axpr.net) typed this ...
> Kurt Sussman wrote:
> > I just put a Seagate 5400 RPM 80G drive in my notebook, and it's like a
> > new computer. OOo starts in 12 seconds; it used to take almost 30.
> 
> I've considered getting a bigger/faster hard drive for my laptop
> (Inspiron 2650) so it can last until Apple has x86 iBooks (I want to
> upgrade to an iBook, but don't want to obsolete myself *just that fast*).

I just got the LCD on this Dell replaced (under warrantee!), so it
should last past version 1.0 of thte x86 Macs...

> However, heat is a significant issue with my laptop.  I've fried one
> wifi card (literally).  I'd rather not do more damage to the system than
> is necessary if it is to last another 24-36 months.

My Orinoco Gold cards heat up like crazy, but they did that in my old
Toshiba notebook also. 

> What kind of heat output are you getting with said 5400RPM drive?  Is it
> noticably warmer?  Does it suck down the battery faster?  In your speed
> comparisons, what kind of drive was your old drive?

According to my research, the 5400 RPM drives use power more efficiently
than the 4200 RPM drives. The technology in the older drives hasn't
changed significantly since they switched from 12.5mm to 9.5mm. The 5400
RPM drives, on the other hand, have smarter firmware, bigger caches (so
the heads don't have to move around as much), better bearings (less
power required to maintain rotation), and more efficient electronics.

I haven't noticed any difference in the battery life or heat, and I have
the drive in a more aggressive mode (longer delay before spindown) than
the old drive was using.

> On my system, the small amount of RAM (384MB) and slow hard drive are
> the two significant bottlenecks, both feeding off each other (reduced
> disk caching due to small amount of RAM and slow swapping due to a slow
> hard drive)... I can only upgrade my RAM to 512MB, leaving me with only
> a faster hard drive to speed things up and last me another few years.

What kind of memory do you need? I have a couple of 256M PC2100 cards
here...

--Kurt

From ehem at m5p.com  Sun Aug 21 17:30:49 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Sun, 21 Aug 2005 17:30:49 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Any lispers out there?  Newbie needs booster shot
In-Reply-To: <20050821112021.42C7B4454@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200508220030.j7M0Unm7095876@m5p.com>

>From: Carlos Konstanski <ckonstan at lunarlogic.com>
> Isn't AutoCAD also written in lisp?

Perhaps early versions in the past, but I highly doubt they're using LISP
today. As far as the hardware requirements go though, part of that is
OS overhead, but a lot of it is working with very large amounts of very
detailed data.


> multimethod in C, using structs.  Java doesn't even support multile
> inheritance.  There are implementations of lisp that get parsed and
> rewritten as C, and then compiled.  C doesn't suck like java does.

Whatever else you say about Java, they *did* do a very nice job with the
object system. Making inheritance strictly for code reuse and interfaces
for abstraction works quite well and efficiently. Multiple inheritance
in C++ does get insane quickly.

> But it still has certain shortcomings that are inherent in any
> language with a rigid syntax that distinguishes between functions and
> data.

Distinguishing them is a bad thing? Given that most processors being made
now do tend to make data non-executable, this does seem to map quite well
to C's target, low-level hardware. I'll confess that all too often I wish
there was a more advanced preprocessor, but then again M4 is available.

Failing that, that distinction really isn't /that/ strong. Pointers to
functions are like any other data type. I can easily cast random data to
a function pointer and cause it to be executed. The results will almost
certainly be non-useful, but I can do it. I could also cast a function
pointer to something and use it as data, again not too useful in most
cases, but I can do it.

On Tue, 2 Aug 2005, Doug Carter wrote:
> I'm sorry, I must not have worded my question correctly. Let me try 
> again:
>
> I'm looking for a auto-responding email utility for my users. The 
> "vacation" program was once available and now it's not. I'm looking for 
> an alternative if one is available.
>
> I didn't ask anything about mailing lists, so maybe you've confused my 
> question with a different thread.

Woah!  Look back a bit, buddy.  You asked "What happened to the vacation 
utility?" and Russ offered his opinion as to why he thinks it's no longer 
part of "the standard distribution" (another of your laments).  The 
request for an alternative was the last sentence of your post.

If you ask what to use, you're going to get opinions that include 
"nothing" (usually with an explanation as to why).  Russ's response was 
completely appropriate for your original post.  No confusion on his part.

On Debian (both stable and unstable -- I tried my mail server as well, I 
get this:

jbrelin at person:~$ apt-cache search vacation
bsdmainutils - collection of more utilities from FreeBSD
gnarwl - Email autoresponder based on LDAP
mailagent - An automatic mail-processing tool and filter.
plan - X/Motif day planner (dynamically compiled with LessTif)
sork-accounts - Account handling framework for horde
sork-vacation - Manage vacation notices in horde
vacation - email autoresponder

There are several alternatives.  The original was removed from 
bsdmainutils and now resides in its own package exactly because it is so 
often abused or misused that it's better to give the administrator the 
option of installing it or not.

I don't know why your system doesn't have it.  Perhaps you need a better 
way to browse available packages.

J.

On Mon, Aug 22, 2005 at 04:41:31PM -0700, Roderick A. Anderson wrote:
> I've thought of this but not too sure how it would work.  At several
> thousand to tens of thounds of messages per hour I think I really need (
> or the "Powers That Be" will want ) something more _streamy_.

Uhm, at that point it becomes more appropriate to have a script look
at the logfiles for you.  Monitoring streamy things at that rate is
a great way of inducing fatigue and indifference in the operator.

Unsolicited advice:
Think blinky warning things instead.  Also think LogWatch, NAGIOS,
sendpage and other off-the-shelf tools for making your job easier and
your TPS* reports prettier and more action-packed.

My experience with the Java SSH thingy was that I couldn't find a
Winders box with Java installed anyway.

You may just be best off with PuTTY, or with CygWin ssh.


*Or whatever you call your regular "what you're paying me for" status
report to the higher-ups.

> sendpage and other off-the-shelf tools for making your job easier and
> your TPS* reports prettier and more action-packed.

The scroll is using colorize so we get lots of color blinky things.  When
a support call comes in or a pager goes off we can look and determine 
quickly if messages are being delivered ( bright green 250 mesages  ) to 
the _actual_ mail server etc.

> My experience with the Java SSH thingy was that I couldn't find a
> Winders box with Java installed anyway.

This is probably true here.  The d**n box is close to needing a reload 
according to the WindersFolks.  

> You may just be best off with PuTTY, or with CygWin ssh.

You're probably right.  But since the logs get rotated once a week I have 
to login again and restart ( select from a menu ) the processes and then 
move them around on the Windows box screen so they are viewable/usable.  

I may end up back at writing a custom watchy-log application that has all 
the bells and whistles wanted and needed.


Thanks for the ideas,
Rod

AthlonRob (athlonrob at axpr.net) typed this ...
> Kurt Sussman wrote:
> > I just put a Seagate 5400 RPM 80G drive in my notebook, and it's like a
> > new computer. OOo starts in 12 seconds; it used to take almost 30.
> > 
> > It was about $130 from newegg.com.
> 
> I'm looking at Newegg right now and don't see a Seagate drive in the
> 80GB size... but do see 60GB drives.  Close enough for me.

Sorry, I got the Hitachi. I was going to get the Seagate, but I wanted
80G and there was one other factor that I can't recall right now. I
remembered the Seagate because it scored the best in the reviews I read.
The Hitachi was not far behind.

> However, you stated you did some research before purchase - why'd you go
> with the Seagate as opposed to the Toshiba drives which offer a full
> 16MB of cache at a lower cost?  Is the motor/firmware/whatever in the
> Seagate considerably better or is it just Seagate's good name that was
> the deciding factor?

I was looking for something that would be faster AND keep the power
requirements about the same (or better). The Toshiba didn't benchmark
significantly better in spite of the cache, and I think it was noisier,
though I can't remember exactly what its shortcomings were. I've had
Toshiba drives fail on me in the past, and while my hardware failure
list is not a statistically significant sample, I have not seen failures
in Seagate or IBM/Hitachi drives.

I also got the Hitachi for about $20 less than the 80G drive that scored
better in the power and speed tests. I guess if I had endless money, I
would have gotten the Seagate 100G drive and an extra battery. #:)

--Kurt

was only one comparable drive in the NewEgg catalog that wasn't in the
review at the time I bought.

The review that measured power usage is lost in the neural fog at this
point.  Sorry.

Well, I was time constrained and needed it to deal with a UI issue I
couldn't figure out a sane way of solving otherwise... so I hired a guy
to do it for me.  He pulled it together in a few days and left me with a
framework I was able to relatively easily modify to suit my needs there
and elsewhere.  I haven't taken the time to learn enough Javascript to
really implement it well otherwise.

I wouldn't suggest hiring somebody to do it for you.  :-)

> Ruby On Rails is the way all the cool kids are doing it, but after
> having a go at PHP, I got tired of learning a new language every time
> I write a web-app.

Ditto.  I don't like the look of RoR syntax, either.  It looks too VBish
to me.  I like my curly braces, dammit!

I haven't yet found a decent PHP AJAX framework... and writing AJAX
stuff by hand is quite time consuming when you need to support more than
just IE and Firefox (Opera, Safari, Konqueror, anybody?)...

I'd really like to implement some AJAX on a storefront site I'm writing
(totally from scratch - reinventing a wheel, thankyouverymuch!), but it
just doesn't seem like the necessary browser support is available for it
today.  Oh well..................

From krisa at subtend.net  Tue Aug 23 17:38:45 2005
From: krisa at subtend.net (Kris)
Date: Tue, 23 Aug 2005 17:38:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Dolphin SCI-to-PCI
Message-ID: <20050824003845.GA3082@subtend.net>

I recently became the owner of a used Auspex NS2000 storage array.  The
system controller is two parts: a Tyan MB dual P3 with 3GB of RAM and a
Solaris/SPARC system on a reiser card.  They were connected to each other
using a Dolphin SCI-to-PCI board which has two high density connectors on
it similar to VHDCI SCSI only shorter. 

I'm gutting the system, putting the Mylex ExtremeRAID 2000 controllers in 
a linux box I have, and connecting the disk shelves up to that.  Hardware 
RAID and lvm are working great so far.

The dolphins are available for anyone who wants them.  I only ask that
you make a small donation to the EFF.

The look like this:
http://www.dolphinics.com/products/hardware/pci64.html

-- 
I'm just a packet pusher.

From blitters at gmail.com  Wed Aug 24 11:41:45 2005
From: blitters at gmail.com (Steve D...)
Date: Wed, 24 Aug 2005 11:41:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Network Configs and Software
Message-ID: <ff01ea180508241141227f4284@mail.gmail.com>

  Does anyone know of an OpenSource (or not) application that monitors
the software release and configurations of network devices, like
routers, switches, and CSUs.

  We currently use spreadsheets and a collection of vendor tools.  We
use CiscoWorks to handle Cisco devices.  Other vendor seems to have
their own "Enterprise" tool to monitor their own stuff.  I haven't
found anything that works across different platforms.  My managers
(and auditors) want us to do a better job.

  COSI on SourceForge has some nice tools.  CiscoConf and configsafe
are pretty close.  They look like they could be extended with a little
effort, but my management is unfriendly to me writing lots of code. 
They don't like "science projects".

  Is anyone out there doing anything clever to audit the software and
configs in network device?

Steve D...

Can you get enough information out of SNMP? 
iso.org.dod.internet.mgmt.mib-2.system.sysDescr (1.3.6.1.2.1.1.1)
tends to give quite a bit of info about most switch software revisions
that I've encountered.  The standard snmpget tool from net-snmp-utils
is pretty scriptable.

Jason

Howdy folks,

I'm having a heck of a time getting my SCSI devices (2 hard

different machines.  On one machine I'm using a BusLogic SCSI
card.  On the other I'm using an Adaptec SCSI card.  The BIOS
of both machines recognizes the cards and the attached devices.
Linux on both machines recognizes the SCSI cards, but neither
machine will show any attached SCSI devices.  I used to run
MDK10.0 on the machine that is now MDK10.1, and the SCSI hard
drives worked just fine at that time.  I was running a 2.6 kernel
on MDK10.0, and I'm running 2.6 kernels on both machines now.

I've searched web archives and Linux sites for info on this
trouble.  One person said there was an errata, but they weren't
clear on the version, which might have been MDK10.0.  One person
said that they cured their trouble by moving their SCSI card to
a different PCI slot.  I tried my SCSI card in every PCI slot on
one of the machines.  Others are saying that udev could be the
problem.  I'm not versed in udev at all.  Is there anyone out in
PLUG-land that can give me some advice?

From srau at rauhaus.org  Wed Aug 24 12:11:38 2005
From: srau at rauhaus.org (Stafford A. Rau)
Date: Wed, 24 Aug 2005 12:11:38 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Network Configs and Software
In-Reply-To: <ff01ea180508241141227f4284@mail.gmail.com>
References: <ff01ea180508241141227f4284@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20050824191138.GB9223@rauhaus.org>

* Steve D... <blitters at gmail.com> [050824 11:42]:
>   Does anyone know of an OpenSource (or not) application that monitors
> the software release and configurations of network devices, like
> routers, switches, and CSUs.

RANCID - http://www.shrubbery.net/rancid/. It's great, simple, and at
this point handles Cisco, Juniper, HP, Kentrox, Bay, Foundry,
Riverstone, Redback, Netscreen, and other devices. Uses a combination of
Perl and Expect to telnet or ssh to devices, and stores their configs in
a CVS repository.

Developers are here in town, even. Works great.

--Stafford

From rsteff at comcast.net  Thu Aug 25 09:40:17 2005
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Thu, 25 Aug 2005 09:40:17 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Unknown Document File Types
Message-ID: <430DF471.2040500@comcast.net>

I have a set of files from a Mac using friend, who isn't very 
sophisticated around computers. He composed this set of files a number 
of years ago -- well before OS-X -- and doesn't remember what word 
processor he was using at the time. It appears that there are several 
different file types involved, so he probably used different word 
processors at different times.

Is there a Linux tool available that will identify what was used to 
create a document? As I look at them with Konqueror's file browser, some 
of them are described as text files, some are described as troff files, 
and the rest are described as unknown.

The ones identified as text files do open as text files with Kate, but 
the text is obviously not correct. An example is:

W?walke?alon?tree-line?ban? o?th?Ca?Rive?a?sunset

The ones labeled troff files open with Kate and display some text as 
well as some graphic characters, like a rectangular box, sometimes 
solid, sometimes just an outline, etc. Some of the ones described as 
unknown type by Konqueror open with Kate and display normal text for the 
most part, but some display mostly rectangular boxes.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens
http://home.comcast.net/~rsteff/

Note Cards & Photographic Prints available at:
http://home.comcast.net/~rcsphoto/


From ron at braithwaites.net  Thu Aug 25 09:59:33 2005
From: ron at braithwaites.net (Ron Braithwaite)
Date: Thu, 25 Aug 2005 09:59:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Unknown Document File Types
In-Reply-To: <430DF471.2040500@comcast.net>
References: <430DF471.2040500@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <EBA83A71-CBBE-4C5C-B4CC-0C695EC7BAB8@braithwaites.net>

On Aug 25, 2005, at 9:40 AM, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> The ones identified as text files do open as text files with Kate,  
> but the text is obviously not correct. An example is:
>
> W?walke?alon?tree-line?ban? o?th?Ca?Rive?a?sunset

That very much reminds me of the old Word Star format, where the high  
order bit of the last character of each word was set, thereby  
allowing a very rudimentary justification algorithm to just stuff in  
spaces to fill out the line. I /suspect/ that anything capable of  
reading & translating Word Star files could handle this.

But I've been wrong before.

-Ron

From pm at nellump.ath.cx  Thu Aug 25 11:02:58 2005
From: pm at nellump.ath.cx (Paul Mullen)
Date: Thu, 25 Aug 2005 11:02:58 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Unknown Document File Types
In-Reply-To: <430DF471.2040500@comcast.net>
References: <430DF471.2040500@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20050825180258.GC17214@nellump.ath.cx>

On Thu, Aug 25, 2005 at 09:40:17AM -0700, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> I have a set of files from a Mac using friend, who isn't very 
> sophisticated around computers. He composed this set of files a number 
> of years ago -- well before OS-X -- and doesn't remember what word 
> processor he was using at the time. It appears that there are several 
> different file types involved, so he probably used different word 
> processors at different times.

If my aging memory serves, Mac OS saves the identity of the
application used to create a file, and the actual file type in a
"resource" fork that is essentially a part of the file completely
separate from the data. If you copy a Mac OS file onto a file system
that doesn't understand the HFS "binary" file layout, the resource
fork and all its meta-data are lost. At that point, you'll just have
to use trial and error to figure out which app made the file.

Your best bet would be to find someone with an older Macintosh and an
array of popular Mac OS word processors from back in the old days.
Call up your local elementary school. They're likely to have an old
Mac or two still in service. It's also possible that contemporary OS X
word processing apps might be able to deal with the mystery files.


Paul

From pm at nellump.ath.cx  Thu Aug 25 11:08:48 2005
From: pm at nellump.ath.cx (Paul Mullen)
Date: Thu, 25 Aug 2005 11:08:48 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Unknown Document File Types
In-Reply-To: <20050825180258.GC17214@nellump.ath.cx>
References: <430DF471.2040500@comcast.net>
	<20050825180258.GC17214@nellump.ath.cx>
Message-ID: <20050825180848.GD17214@nellump.ath.cx>

On Thu, Aug 25, 2005 at 11:02:58AM -0700, Paul Mullen wrote:
> 
> If my aging memory serves, Mac OS saves the identity of the
> application used to create a file, and the actual file type in a
> "resource" fork that is essentially a part of the file completely
> separate from the data.

Yikes. That prose doesn't read as well as it typed. Trying again:

Mac OS files traditionally have two separate "forks", a data fork, and
a resource fork. The resource fork is used to store meta-data and such
about the file, and this includes a reference to both the application
that created the file (the "creator" resource) and the file's "type"
(word processing document, spreadsheet, etc.). If you copy that Mac OS
file from the native HFS file system to any of the usual Unix/DOS file
systems without taking special precautions, then the resource fork and
all its meta-data will be lost.


Paul

From pm at nellump.ath.cx  Thu Aug 25 12:39:48 2005
From: pm at nellump.ath.cx (Paul Mullen)
Date: Thu, 25 Aug 2005 12:39:48 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Unknown Document File Types

	<20050825180848.GD17214@nellump.ath.cx>
	<430E0CEC.2070808@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20050825193948.GA17800@nellump.ath.cx>

On Thu, Aug 25, 2005 at 11:24:44AM -0700, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> 
> Then, I should be able to have my friend look at the files on his
> Mac and figure out that info (assuming he can understand what I'm
> asking of him). He's in Texas, so it's all being done by e-mail.

It might not be that simple. If he doesn't have the original
application installed, the Finder may just show the file as an unknown
type. If memory serves, the "classic" Finder doesn't show the raw
creator and type values. You need a special utility for that. Have him
take a look at FileTyper:

<http://dazuma.freeshell.org/filetyper/>

It's a drag-n-drop app, so it should be straightforward to use. Once
he figures out the creator and type codes, you'll need to determine
what they actually mean. Sometimes it's obvious, but often not (both
codes are only four bytes, so some creativity was occasionally
necessary to maintain uniqueness).


Paul

From seniorr at aracnet.com  Fri Aug 26 15:22:41 2005
From: seniorr at aracnet.com (Russell Senior)
Date: 26 Aug 2005 15:22:41 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] determining files associated with particular sectors?

	<1125074577.7277.14.camel@eel.myhealthbank.com>

>>>>> "Wil" == Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc> writes:

Russell> Is there a utility that will tell me, given a sector number
Russell> within a device, which file is allocated to that sector?  I
Russell> am seeing a few bad sectors on the old laptop drive (as I
Russell> copy the files over) and want to ensure I patch up any
Russell> associated bitrot.

Wil> I think debugfs will tell you that; it seems to be able to tell
Wil> you pretty much everything else you rarely need to know...

Here's how I managed to do it:

 a) I had a list of device sectors from /var/log/messages;

 b) from /sbin/fdisk, changing units to sectors, I got the sector
    offset of /dev/hdc3 and filesystem size in sectors

 c) /sbin/e2fsck -n -v /dev/hdc3 gave me the filesystem size in
    blocks, which let me deduce the block-size, which gave me a way to
    translate from /dev/hdc sector numbers to filesystem block
    numbers;

 d) from /sbin/debugfs, I used the icheck command with the list of
    block numbers, which gave me one consistent inode;

 e) also in /sbin/debugfs I used the ncheck command to list pathnames
    that point at the inode.

Turns out it was a kernel source tarball, which is easily replaced.

From 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com  Fri Aug 26 15:35:00 2005
From: 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com (Steve Bonds)
Date: Fri, 26 Aug 2005 15:35:00 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] determining files associated with particular sectors?
In-Reply-To: <868xypi13e.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <868xypi13e.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <29283-03649@sneakemail.com>

On 26 Aug 2005 00:55:33 -0700, Russell Senior seniorr-at-aracnet.com
|PDX Linux| <...> wrote:
> 
> Is there a utility that will tell me, given a sector number within a
> device, which file is allocated to that sector?  I am seeing a few bad
> sectors on the old laptop drive (as I copy the files over) and want to
> ensure I patch up any associated bitrot.

Russell:

Most disk forensics programs will do this.  Since the disk is damaged
you'll need to use "dd" with a couple options as described here:

http://www.forensicfocus.com/index.php?name=Forums&file=viewtopic&t=29

Summary:

dd if=/dev/broken-disk-device of=broken-disk.img conv=noerror,sync

A good forensics package is The Sleuth Kit:

http://www.sleuthkit.org/

This will operate on the "dd"-ed image of your disk and let you dig
around through the mess to attempt file recovery without putting the
original at risk.

  -- Steve

New Releases
----------------------------------------------------------------
***FrontPage 2003: The Missing Manual
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 059600950X
This book puts FrontPage 2003's features in context, with clear and
thorough chapters that provide valuable shortcuts, workarounds, and just
plain common sense, no matter where you weigh in on the technical scale.
You will learn to build web pages, simple or sophisticated, and find out
how to manage and publish your website. You'll also learn to create
forms, work with databases, and integrate FrontPage with Microsoft
Office.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/frontpagetmm/

Chapter 7, "Cascading Style Sheets," is available online:
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/frontpagetmm/chapter/index.html


***No Nonsense XML Web Development with PHP
Publisher: SitePoint
ISBN: 097524020X
Practical and concise, this book teaches XML from the ground up and
explains how XML can be put to use in real-world projects. Written in a
tutorial style, it presents various XML methodologies and techniques in
an easy-to-understand way, building a basis for further exploration.
This book also covers buzz topics such as RSS and Web Services.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/097524020X/


***Eclipse IDE Pocket Guide
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596100655
This practical pocket guide covers basic Eclipse concepts as well as
features that are not commonly understood, such as Perspectives and
Launch Configurations. You'll learn how to write and debug your Java
code and how to integrate that code with tools such as Ant and JUnit.
You'll also get a collection of tips and tricks to handle common tasks
in your Java development cycle.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/eclipsepg/

Chapter 6, "Tips and Tricks," is available online:
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/eclipsepg/chapter/index.html


***The Unofficial LEGO Builder's Guide
Publisher: No Starch Press
ISBN: 1593270542
In "The Unofficial LEGO Builder's Guide," readers will discover
everything from how to craft sturdy walls and a basic sphere to more
advanced concepts such as scale and design. Illustrations demonstrate
construction tips that can be applied to a wide variety of original
creations made from real bricks. Includes essential terminology and an
overview of different types of LEGO pieces.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/1593270542/index.html


***XML Pocket Reference, 3rd Edition
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596100507
This perennial bestseller has been revised once again to give you quick
access to the latest goods. In addition to its comprehensive look at
XML, this third edition has been updated with new material on Namespaces
and XML Schema, along with RELAX NG and Schematron. Featuring a
well-organized format that gets right to the point, this compact book is
perfect for getting XML answers quick and on the fly.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/xmlpr3/


***Network Security Evaluation Using the NSA IEM
Publisher: Syngress
ISBN: 1597490350
Finally, a book that gives you everything you need to provide the most
comprehensive technical security posture evaluation for any
organization. The NSA's recommended methodology is described in depth,
leading you through each step in providing customers with analysis
customized to their organization. From setting scope and legal
coordination to the final report and trending metrics, this book has it
all.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/1597490350/index.html


***Fast Guide to Propellerhead Reason, 2nd Edition
Publisher: PC Publishing
ISBN: 1870775937
This in-depth guide takes you through every separate Reason device as
well as all the devices and changes introduced with the V2.5 update.
Every control and function is clearly explained. In addition, standard
and exotic techniques are introduced at the points where you will find
them most useful, and step-by-step programming tutorials help increase
your hands-on skills with Reason.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/1870775937/


***Oracle DBA Pocket Guide
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596100493
This handy reference is designed to help administrators make more
effective use of their time by presenting a compact summary of DBA tasks
in an easy-to-use form. With this book by your side, you'll have instant
access to the most important concepts, best practices, tips, and
checklists. Key topics include architecture, installation,
configuration, tuning, and backup/recovery.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/oracledbapg/


***Windows XP Cookbook
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596007256
Useful to anyone that has to use, deploy, administer, or automate
Windows XP, this handy reference guide provides practical solutions for
the most common Windows XP tasks. The over 300 step-by-step recipes
enable you to install, manage, and support your operating system with
ease. This book also covers Microsoft's XP Service Pack 2.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/windowsxpckbk/

Chapter 6, "System Properties, Startup, and Shutdown," is available
online:
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/windowsxpckbk/chapter/index.html


***Digital Identity
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596008783
This fascinating book shows how an enterprise-wide "identity management
architecture" (IMA) can provide security while ensuring that
interactions with customers, employees, partners, and suppliers are
richer and more flexible. Through a detailed, real-world view of the
concepts, issues, and technologies behind IMA, this book shows CIOs,
other IT professionals, product managers, and programmers how security
planning can support business goals and opportunities rather than
holding them at bay.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/digidentity/

Chapter 13, "An Architecture for Digital Identity," is available online:
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/digidentity/chapter/index.html


***Host Integrity Monitoring Using Osiris and Samhain
Publisher: Syngress
ISBN: 1597490180
Host Integrity Monitoring is the most effective way to determine if some
form of malicious attack or threat has compromised your network security
to modify the filesystem, system configuration, or runtime environment
of monitored hosts. This book provides foundation information on host
integrity monitoring as well as specific, detailed instruction on using
best of breed products Osiris and Samhain.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/1597490180/index.html


***Agile Web Development with Rails
Publisher: Pragmatic
ISBN: 097669400X
This book shows you how easy it is to install Rails using your web
server of choice. In the extended tutorial sections, you'll create a
complete online store application to see how a full Rails application is
developed. Further, you'll learn how to exploit the Rails service
frameworks to send emails, implement web services, and create dynamic,
user-centric web pages. There are also extensive chapters on testing,
deployment, and scaling.
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/097669400X/

General News
---------------------
***MAKE: Blog is Nominated for "Best of the Web" on Business Week
Cast your vote today and help us make number one.
http://www.businessweek.com/technology/bestofweb.htm


***Visualizing the O'Reilly Connection Network Using FOAF and Graphviz
Timothy M. O'Brien gathered some interesting imagery and offered insight
into the growing network in the O'Reilly Connection. Just over a month
old and the network already looks as busy as the air traffic pattern
over O'Hare.
http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/wlg/7631


***What is AdSense
Looking for ways to generate some cash for that website you've been
developing? Google's AdSense may be your answer. This introduction to
AdSense will help you decide if the program, which allows you to sell
advertising space for other people's ads on your own site, is right for
you.
http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/network/2005/07/26/WhatIsAdSense.html


***SafariU: Create Your Own Textbook in No Time
With the SafariU web-based service, you can build your own custom
textbook or online syllabus in a few hours drawing from the extensive
list of top technical titles and 5,000 plus article database. Your
printed custom book can be on local bookstore shelves in as little as
two weeks. The online syllabus and electronic version of your book are
accessible immediately. The service is free to qualified instructors.
Visit SafariU to sign up for access.
https://www.safariu.com/


***Learning Lab August Special
In our practice-based, self-paced courses, you can build your online
portfolio with plenty of instructor feedback and a free O'Reilly book
for reference. For a limited time, use the discount passcode "tarsier"
to save an extra 15% off any of our courses--including all University of
Illinois Certificate Series. 
http://oreilly.useractive.com/courses/certificates.php3


***Identity Management Architectures and Digital Identity 
Building an identity management infrastructure requires a strategy; one
that takes into account not only the technology, but the politics and
economics surrounding digital identity. Phil Windley calls such a
strategy an identity management architecture, or IMA. Here, he defines
what an IMA is, and discusses the key components and myths to developing
one. Phil is the author of O'Reilly's "Digital Identity."
http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/network/2005/08/19/digitaldentity.html

---------------------
Open Source
---------------------
***Linux for Video Production 
Linux and open source software is traditionally good for developers and
system administrators, and recently good for business users. When will
it be good for multimedia users? A handful of projects are making video
production and editing possible (and useful). Jono Bacon examines the
present and future of video production with Linux and open source
software. Jono is the coauthor of "Linux Desktop Hacks."
http://www.linuxdevcenter.com/pub/a/linux/2005/08/18/linux_video.html


***How to Decide What Bugs to Fix When, Part 1 
There are two challenges to making smart bug decisions: first,
understanding how to make good bug-fix decisions; and second, creating
and following rules that make it easy to stick to those decisions when
the pressure is high. In this first installment of a two-part essay,
Scott Berkun, author of "The Art of Project Management," provides the
core ideas you need to make your own bug-fixing rules.
http://www.onlamp.com/pub/a/onlamp/2005/08/11/fixingbugs.html


***PHP 6.0 Ingredients
A wish-list of PHP 6.0 features leads to controversy among some PHP
users and Web Hosts. Find out what Tom Rutter thinks of the wish list
and what he thinks is missing.
http://www.sitepoint.com/blogs/2005/08/15/php-60-ingredients/

---------------------
Mac
---------------------
***Web Apps with Tiger: Getting Started 
Morbus is back with more web serving tools and tricks, updated for Mac
OS X Tiger. In this first article, he'll take you on a whirlwind tour
through the basics: turning on the Apache web server, learning a teensy
bit of its configuration, then enabling and testing PHP.
http://www.macdevcenter.com/pub/a/mac/2005/08/16/apache.html


***Build a Simple 3D Pipeline in Tcl 
Are you interested in playing with 3D graphics for games? In this
article, Michael Norton shows you how to assemble a game console to
experiment with using Tcl, which is a great tool for playing with
graphics algorithms.
http://www.macdevcenter.com/pub/a/mac/2005/08/12/tcl.html


***Turn Your Old Mac into a Home-automation System
Automating your home--so the lights turn on automatically when darkness
falls, the heat turns on a half an hour before you're due home, or a
security camera watches the house while you're out--isn't that hard to
do. Gordon Meyer, author of "Smart Home Hacks," tells you how.
http://www.macworld.com/2005/08/features/oldmacnewtricks3/index.php

---------------------
Windows/.NET
---------------------
***What Is Visual Studio 
What can you really do with Visual Studio? James Avery discusses some of
the various applications you can build using Visual Studio, some of its
most compelling development features, and what you need to know to get
started writing quality applications in Visual Studio. James is the
author of "Visual Studio Hacks."
http://www.windowsdevcenter.com/pub/a/windows/2005/08/22/
whatisVisualStudio.html


***Setting Up Vonage with Your PC 
If you're looking to save money on phone calls and get extra VoIP
features, Vonage is a good bet. Russell Shaw shows you how to set up
Vonage with your PC.
http://www.windowsdevcenter.com/pub/a/windows/2005/08/23/Vonage.html

---------------------
Java
---------------------
***Using Drools in Your Enterprise Java Application 
Enterprise Java developers have many fine framework choices at the
presentation and persistence levels, but what about the business logic
that sits in the middle? Do you want to recompile a mass of if...then
spaghetti code every time a manager drops a new gotcha in your lap? In
this article, Paul Browne suggests that a rule engine like Drools may be
an ideal fit for this task.
http://www.onjava.com/pub/a/onjava/2005/08/24/drools.html


***Hacking Swing with Undocumented Classes and Properties 
Joshua Marinacci, coauthor of "Swing Hacks," shows you six undocumented
features, classes, and properties that let you hack into Swing. From how
to hide a frame from the Windows task bar to how to make Mac OS X
windows truly transparent, these undocumented hacks can add a level of
polish that will make your apps stand out from the rest.
http://www.onjava.com/pub/a/onjava/2005/08/10/swinghacks.html

---------------------
Digital Media
---------------------
***Ten Tips for Improving Your Podcasts
Jack Herrington, author of "Podcasting Hacks," offers his top ten
suggestions for creating great podcasts. He starts with the basics:
reducing noise, getting a good microphone, proper microphone technique,
show prep, and format, and closes with tips that deal with improving the
content of your show.
http://digitalmedia.oreilly.com/2005/08/10/improvingpodcasts.html


***Get Cooking With Photoshop and CSS
With Photoshop, CSS, and a little creativity, you can enjoy a feast of
design options. Corrie shows how easy it is to tweak the graphic
ingredients of your designs to produce completely different results. She
cooks up three tasty design styles in Photoshop, then shows,
step-by-step, how to reproduce them using CSS.
http://www.sitepoint.com/article/get-cooking-photoshop-css


***Mobile Video: Working with MPEG-4 Clips on Mobile Phones
MPEG-4 files can be struggle to work with, but the format is so good
it's worth taming. In this article, Douglas Dixon uses the QuickTime
Player to view and deconstruct clips created by several camera phones.
He examines the details of the MPEG-4 format for mobile phones--called
3GPP--and works around some of the idiosyncrasies of different devices 
that create slightly different formats.
http://digitalmedia.oreilly.com/2005/08/24/mpeg4.html

---------------------
MAKE
---------------------
***MAKE: Audio--Natalie Jeremijenko
MAKE: Audio rolls out a new type of show: A MAKER profile of Natalie
Jeremijenko, who builds toxic-sensing robot dogs from discarded toys, as
read by Dale Dougherty, MAKE publisher. This is an enhanced podcast; it
will play audio *and* show the actual pages of MAKE Magazine from volume
02 when you click on them. We hope to do more of these--please let us
know what you think.

Don't forget to add the MAKE feed to iTunes 4.9. Click this link:
http://phobos.apple.com/WebObjects/MZStore.woa/wa/viewPodcast?id=74069835
and click SUBSCRIBE.


***How to Get RSS Feeds on Your Watch
Phil Torrone show's us how to get MSN filter content or any news via
RSS on a SPOT watch:
http://www.makezine.com/blog/archive/2005/08/how_to_get_msn.html#more

You can also get MAKE shows via ODEO!
http://odeo.com/channel/1178/view

MAKE Show Archive:
http://www.makezine.com/blog/archive/make_podcast/

http://www.makezine.com/

                             MEETING ANNOUNCEMENT

                        The Portland Linux/Unix Group
                                  will meet
                          7 PM Thursday Sep 1, 2005
                                      at
                          Portland State University
                                      in
                                 Cramer Hall
                                    Room 1
                           On the block bounded by
         SW Montgomery, SW Broadway (7th), SW Mill, and SW Park (9th)
          (Note: Smith Memorial Center is still under construction.)
      *******************************************************************
          
                                 PRESENTATION

                         Introduction to Web Mapping

                                      by

                                 David Mandel
                            <dmandel at pdxLinux.org>

      David Mandel will discuss the nuts and bolts of a web site he is
      currently building using UMN MapServer.  Topics will include:

          -  Overview of a few GIS concepts
             Why GIS is different than other graphics.
             Why Web mapping is different than other web applications.

          -  Overview of UMN MapServer
             What it will do and what it won't do.
             Brief discussion of alternative products.

          -  A critical view of a few cool web mapping sites

          -  A quick look at the site David Mandel is currently working 
on.
             It is definitely a work in progress, but already has some
             interesting features.

From ckuskie at dalsemi.com  Tue Aug 30 10:43:06 2005
From: ckuskie at dalsemi.com (Colin Kuskie)
Date: Tue, 30 Aug 2005 10:43:06 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Problems using USB to serial adapter
Message-ID: <20050830174306.GI27745@dalsemi.com>

Normally, when I have hardware problems in Linux, it's because the
device isn't supported.  I seem to have moved on to a new stage.

At work, we use a serial device to communicate with the chips we
design.  Since my laptop doesn't have a serial port, I bought
a USB to serial adapter, based on the Prolific PL2303 chipset.

The USB adapter seems to be dropping bytes.  For example,
when I send this sequence of bytes (character codes):

23,69,91,15,145

I should get back:

22,68,90,0,147

However, through the USB adapter it will usually drop 1 or 2 of
the beginning bytes:

68,90,0,147

90,0,147

This same setup works fine on a box with a dedicated serial port.

I've tried googling, and am searching through the 1004 results,
but so far I haven't come across anything that looks remotely
similar to what I see.

Has anyone on the list used one of these things, or have suggestions
for debugging further?

Thanks,
Colin

OK, I'm confused...

That's not unusual, but I thought I had my postfix UCE setup pretty well 
understood.

I have the following entries in /etc/postfix/main.cf:

smtpd_client_restrictions = hash:/etc/postfix/access,
        reject_rbl_client dnsbl.sorbs.net,
        reject_rbl_client list.dsbl.org,
        reject_rbl_client opm.blitzed.org,
        reject_rbl_client relays.ordb.org,
        reject_rbl_client sbl.spamhaus.org,
        reject_rbl_client dnsbl.njabl.org,
        reject_rbl_client blackhole.securitysage.com,
        reject_rbl_client cbl.abuseat.org,
        reject_rbl_client bl.spamcop.net,
        reject_rbl_client china.blackholes.us,
        reject_rbl_client taiwan.blackholes.us,
        reject_rbl_client japan.blackholes.us,
        reject_rbl_client korea.blackholes.us,
        reject_rbl_client nigeria.blackholes.us,
        reject_rbl_client argentina.blackholes.us,
        reject_rbl_client brazil.blackholes.us,
        reject_rbl_client thailand.blackholes.us,
        reject_rbl_client russia.blackholes.us

#smtpd_sender_restrictions = 
reject_unknown_sender_domain,reject_non_fqdn_sender
,reject_maps_rbl
smtpd_sender_restrictions = reject_rbl_client dnsbl.sorbs.net,
        reject_rbl_client list.dsbl.org,
        reject_rbl_client opm.blitzed.org,
        reject_rbl_client relays.ordb.org,
        reject_rbl_client sbl.spamhaus.org,
        reject_rbl_client dnsbl.njabl.org,
        reject_rbl_client blackhole.securitysage.com,
        reject_rbl_client cbl.abuseat.org
maps_rbl_reject_code = 571

---8<--cut here

Both client restrictions and sender restrictions list cbl.abuseat.org.

How come I'm still getting mail from folks listed at cbl.abuseat.org?

I've received three UCE in the last 6 hours from the same IP, and each 
time, I forward it to spamcop, and it tells me that the source of the 
mail is listed in cbl.abuseat.org and that it's an open relay.

It's not like my setup isn't working. Postfix is rejecting over 50% of 
the attempted email deliveries per day. I look through the output of 
postfixlogsum daily, and see hundreds of rejections, including many that 
are rejected using cbl.abuseat.org..

So I'm really confused now.

Any ideas out there?

Russ

I probably have no useful information to offer but I did get a Belkin brand 
USB to DB9 cable last week at CompUSA and used it with my Garmin GPS. I had 
to install a driver under WinXP to get it working and it worked fine to allow 
me to upgrade the firmware and internal map using Garmin's update programs.

Under SuSE 9.2 it appears that the cable is supported as the cable was 
detected when I plugged it in to a USB port on my laptop. I changed my GPS to 
output NEMA data and I was able to use "gpsdrive" under Linux and it was able 
to receive the data that my GPS was sending out. The GPS appears to only 
support 4800 bps so I'm not doing any high speed data transfers under Linux.

One thing I do notice is that the "setserial" program doesn't work with 
USB-serial ports so I don't know how I check the speed and flow control under 
Linux with this cable. The cable is mapped to /dev/ttyUSB0. 

Tony

Anthony Schlemmer

From plug at martinconsulting.com  Wed Aug 31 11:40:33 2005
From: plug at martinconsulting.com (Brian Martin)
Date: Wed, 31 Aug 2005 11:40:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] 15Mb Access
Message-ID: <20050831184037.54802136AC@mail.silverflash.net>

I'm now the proud user of a fiber optic line to my house providing 15Mb/sec
of Internet wonders and mysteries ... except that I can't get 15Mb out of
it.  The technician only knew Windows, so we tested the line with a Windows
box.  It initially measured out at about 5Mb, which he said is typical for
an untuned system.  We then went to speedguide.net, which through the
miracle of Active-X (as I recall) retuned my TCP stack and the box then
measured out at 15Mb.

But, of course, I don't used a Windows firewall.  When I run tests from my
Linux firewall, it tests out at 5Mb again.  My initial research lead me to
RWIN aka RecvWin apparently aka /proc/sys/net/core/rmem_default and
rmem_max.  I set these up to 513920, a number recommended by speedguide.net,
but subsequent tests showed no change.  Speedguide.net still reports RWIN is
5840.  Firefox's bandwidth tester extension confirms that I'm still at
~5Mb/sec.

My question to the collective experience and knowledge of PLUG is: what do I
need to tune on Linux to use the full capability of my line?

          -Brian

From llywrch at agora.rdrop.com  Wed Aug 31 13:40:29 2005
From: llywrch at agora.rdrop.com (Geoff Burling)
Date: Wed, 31 Aug 2005 13:40:29 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] 15Mb Access
In-Reply-To: <20050831184037.54802136AC@mail.silverflash.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.33.0508311326520.28438-100000@joan.burling.com>

On Wed, 31 Aug 2005, Brian Martin wrote:

> I'm now the proud user of a fiber optic line to my house providing 15Mb/sec
> of Internet wonders and mysteries ... except that I can't get 15Mb out of
> it.  The technician only knew Windows, so we tested the line with a Windows
> box.  It initially measured out at about 5Mb, which he said is typical for
> an untuned system.  We then went to speedguide.net, which through the
> miracle of Active-X (as I recall) retuned my TCP stack and the box then
> measured out at 15Mb.
>
> But, of course, I don't used a Windows firewall.  When I run tests from my
> Linux firewall, it tests out at 5Mb again.  My initial research lead me to
> RWIN aka RecvWin apparently aka /proc/sys/net/core/rmem_default and
> rmem_max.  I set these up to 513920, a number recommended by speedguide.net,
> but subsequent tests showed no change.  Speedguide.net still reports RWIN is
> 5840.  Firefox's bandwidth tester extension confirms that I'm still at
> ~5Mb/sec.
>
> My question to the collective experience and knowledge of PLUG is: what do I
> need to tune on Linux to use the full capability of my line?
>
At first glance, I don't see any reason why this connection should be
so slow. Think of it this way: what kind of thruput would you expect from
a 10 Mbit Ethernet connection before tweaking?

But more to your problem, Brian. What's on the other end? Based on your
test with a Winderz box, it doesn't seem likely that the bottleneck's
there -- but it might be some cheap-ass switch that's designed to work
best with that other OS.

What network card are you using with this fiber line? Did the driver
come pre-compiled from your ISP, or did you compile it for your system?
When I did testing at Intel, most of their drivers accepted flags when
insmoded that would tune the performance, & maybe this card needs some
special setting to work with the equipment at the other end. (And I
know that you're bright enough to take this clue & use it in other
obvious ways of troubleshooting. I won't insult your intelligence
by naming them.)

I'd suggest that you try to connect this to an identical card & see
if, with no other changes you can't get 15Mb thruput -- but from the
sounds of things, this might just be some odd specialized (as in
paying lots of money for less benefits) NIC, & there's no sense in
spending that kind of dough just to learn that 5Mb may be all that
the card currently is capable of doing under Linux.

Post any interesting results, & one of us will have more ideas.

Geoff

On 8/31/05, Brian Martin wrote:

> But, of course, I don't used a Windows firewall.  When I run tests from my
> Linux firewall, it tests out at 5Mb again.  My initial research lead me to
> RWIN aka RecvWin apparently aka /proc/sys/net/core/rmem_default and
> rmem_max.  I set these up to 513920, a number recommended by speedguide.net,
> but subsequent tests showed no change.  Speedguide.net still reports RWIN is
> 5840.  Firefox's bandwidth tester extension confirms that I'm still at
> ~5Mb/sec.
> 
> My question to the collective experience and knowledge of PLUG is: what do I
> need to tune on Linux to use the full capability of my line?

I think you were on the right track with the TCP window size tuning,
however only tuning the receive side isn't helpful if you're the one
initiating the TCP connection.

This site offers some excellent advice on tuning the send buffers and
even describes why this is sometimes necessary:

http://web.archive.org/web/20041105011210/http://www.psc.edu/networking/projects/tcptune/

(archive.org link for the benefit of future searchers)

  -- Steve

From 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com  Wed Aug 31 16:05:39 2005
From: 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com (Steve Bonds)
Date: Wed, 31 Aug 2005 16:05:39 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] 15Mb Access
In-Reply-To: <431633FE.30306@schluting.com>
References: <20050831184037.54802136AC@mail.silverflash.net>
	<1125527555.7633.12.camel@eel.myhealthbank.com>
	<431633FE.30306@schluting.com>
Message-ID: <28095-75614@sneakemail.com>

On 8/31/05, Charlie Schluting wrote:

> You can transfer > 15Mb/s on your LAN without tweaking right?

The site I mentioned in my earlier E-mail discusses why TCP windows
that work great on a local LAN may not work so well on longer distance
connections.  (see the "bandwidth-delay product" section)

http://web.archive.org/web/20041105011210/http://www.psc.edu/networking/projects/tcptune/

Basically the buffers need to be big enough to contain all the data
"in flight".  The faster the data gets to its destination, the smaller
the buffers can be.  On a local LAN, data gets to its destination
quite fast, so these buffers can be very small without affecting
throughput.

  -- Steve


From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Wed Aug 31 16:14:21 2005
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Wed, 31 Aug 2005 16:14:21 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] 15Mb Access
In-Reply-To: <431633FE.30306@schluting.com>
References: <20050831184037.54802136AC@mail.silverflash.net>
	<1125527555.7633.12.camel@eel.myhealthbank.com>
	<431633FE.30306@schluting.com>
Message-ID: <1125530061.7633.32.camel@eel.myhealthbank.com>

On Wed, 2005-08-31 at 15:49 -0700, Charlie Schluting wrote:

> .. we're only talking 15Mb/s. Default MTU (btw, MTU doesn't matter.. the 
> other end probably won't talk to you past the default size anyways) is 
> enough, default Linux TCP windows are enough..
> You can transfer > 15Mb/s on your LAN without tweaking right?

But we're not talking about a LAN; we're talking about broadband that
happens to terminate in some piece of routing or bridging equipment that
offers Ethernet on the customer-facing side.  Broadband tends to get
encapsulated and aggregated at layer 2 using things like PPPoE, ATM,
etc.  I'm not talking tuning the kernel itself for handling high
bandwidth; I'm talking about tuning the kernel to operate as efficiently
with the adjoining layer 2 infrastructure.  Here are some resources:

http://www.pengbo.dsl.pipex.com/ppages/tweaks.shtml
http://www.dslreports.com/faq/6266
http://www.psc.edu/networking/projects/tcptune/
http://www.dslreports.com/forum/remark,14068968?hilite=tcpwindowsize

In fact, DSL Reports now has a whole section in its forums about Verizon
Fiber:

http://www.dslreports.com/forum/vzfiber

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd. <http://nakedape.cc>

From slinkywizard at integraonline.com  Wed Aug 31 17:21:38 2005
From: slinkywizard at integraonline.com (slinkymax0r)
Date: Wed, 31 Aug 2005 17:21:38 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] 15Mb Access
In-Reply-To: <20050831184037.54802136AC@mail.silverflash.net>
References: <20050831184037.54802136AC@mail.silverflash.net>
Message-ID: <4776.64.122.164.5.1125534098.squirrel@64.122.164.5>



1) Make sure there are no problems w/ Duplex Mismatch with the NIC on your
firewall and your switch /router.

2) Test Your Firewall Locally against another system on your lan, using
NFS preferably, to verify that you can get that amount of throughput
bi-directionally over ethernet. This little sanity check might save you a
lot of grief.

3) If you can get that amount of throughput on a LAN, but not on the WAN,
I would test against an FTP server on the int4rweb while running tcpdump.

I'm assuming that you're running the tests directly from the firewall, and
not from clients behind the firewall.  If that is not the case, let me
know.
